Hanafi Definitions of Ma'rifa and Nakira: The Primacy of Wad'
Provides and critiques definitions of definite and indefinite nouns.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define ma'rifa and nakira according to the Hanafi usul preference based on wad', distinguish these definitions from alternatives based on context or listener knowledge, and apply them to classify nouns in legal texts.

Core Idea
Definiteness and indefiniteness are rooted in the word's coinage for specificity, not in speaker context or hearer knowledge.

This passage from a Hanafi usul commentary sharpens the definitions of ma'rifa (definite noun) and nakira (indefinite noun). The commentator notes that earlier definitions only covered partial categories, such as 'the mentally familiar' for definite or 'used for an individual' for indefinite. He then presents a more comprehensive definition: the definite noun is coined to be used for a specific thing, the indefinite for a non-specific thing. This lesson will guide you through this definitional refinement and its implications for legal interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions being critiqued
The passage refers to the matn's definitions: the indefinite noun is what is used for the individual without the essence itself; the definite noun includes the 'mentally familiar' (al-mahdud al-dhihni). These are partial.
The matn's definitions are found in earlier usul works; they rely on usage context.
2
Understand the commentator's rationale
The commentator argues that the best definition is based on the word's original coinage (wad'). He rejects reliance on 'unrestricted usage' (itlaq) or the listener's understanding because these are accidental.
Hanafi usul prioritizes wad' as the primary determinant of meaning.
3
State the preferred definitions
The definite noun (ma'rifa) is what was coined to be used for a specific thing (shay' bi-'aynihi). The indefinite noun (nakira) is what was coined to be used for a non-specific thing (shay' la bi-'aynihi).
Specificity is built into the coinage, not context-dependent.
4
Apply the definitions to examples
Consider the word 'al-rajul' (the man): it is collected for a specific man? Actually 'al-rajul' with alif-lam is definite by coinage for a specific man? In Hanafi, common nouns with alif-lam are definite by the letter, but the definition applies more to proper nouns. However, the principle holds: 'Zayd' is definite by coinage; 'rajul' is indefinite unless made definite by alif-lam.
The key is that definiteness is inherent in the word's design, not in usage.
5
Consider the impact on legal interpretation
When a word appears in a legal text, its definiteness or indefiniteness affects whether it is general ('amm) or particular (khass). For example, the indefinite 'raqabah' (neck) in an expiation verse is general for any slave; the definite 'al-raqabah' would refer to a specific one if context demands.
This definitional clarity is essential for correct istinbat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ فَهِيَ مَا وُضِعَ لَمَّا كَانَ الْخَارِجُ مِنَ التَّقْسِيمِ بَعْضَ أَنْوَاعِ النَّكِرَةِ وَهُوَ مَا اسْتُعْمِلَ فِي الْفَرْدِ دُونَ نَفْسِ الْمُسَمَّى وَفِي مُقَابَلَتِهِ بَعْضُ أَقْسَامِ الْمَعْرِفَةِ وَهُوَ الْمَعْهُودُ الذَّهَنِيُّ أَوْرَدَ تَعْرِيفَيِ الْمَعْرِفَةِ ، وَالنَّكِرَةِ عَلَى مَا يَشْتَمِلُ الْأَقْسَامَ كُلَّهَا . قَوْلُهُ عِنْدَ الْإِطْلَاقِ لِلسَّامِعِ ) قَيْدَانِ لِلتَّعْيِينِ وَعَدَمِهِ وَالْأَحْسَنُ فِي تَعْرِيفِهِمَا مَا قِيلَ : إِنَّ الْمَعْرِفَةَ مَا وُضِعَ لِيُسْتَعْمَلَ فِي شَيْءٍ بِعَيْنِهِ وَالنَّكِرَةَ مَا وُضِعَ لِيُسْتَعْمَلَ فِي شَيْءٍ لَا بِعَيْنِهِ ، فَالْمُعْتَبَرُ فِي التَّعْيِينِ وَعَدَمِهِ أَنْ يَكُونَ ذَلِكَ بِحَسَبِ دَلَالَةِ اللَّفْظِ ، وَلَا عِبْرَةَ بِحَالَةِ الْإِطْلَاقِ دُونَ الْوَضْعِ وَلَا بِمَا عِنْدَ السَّامِعِ دُونَ الْمُتَكَلِّمِ
His statement: 'For it (the indefinite noun) is what was coined...' Since what came out of the division is some types of the indefinite noun, namely what is used for the individual without the essence itself, and in its counterpart is some sections of the definite noun, namely the mentally familiar (al-mahdud al-dhihni), he presented the two definitions of definite and indefinite nouns as covering all sections. His statement: 'upon unrestricted usage for the hearer' — these are two conditions for determination and non-determination. And the best in their definition is what was said: 'The definite is what was coined to be used for a specific thing, and the indefinite is what was coined to be used for a non-specific thing.' So the consideration in determination and non-determination is that it be according to the indication of the word, and no regard is paid to the state of unrestricted usage (atlaq) without coinage (wad'), nor to what the hearer understands without the speaker.
Source pages p. 528

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definitions of ma'rifa (definite noun) and nakira (indefinite noun) in Hanafi usul. The commentator notes that the matn's definition only covered some types (like mental familiarity for definite and individual usage for indefinite), so he provides a more comprehensive definition: The definite noun is coined to be used for a specific (mu'ayyan) thing; the indefinite noun is coined to be used for a non-specific thing. This definition relies on the word's original coinage (wad'), not on the context of unrestricted usage (itlaq) or the hearer's understanding. The key principle: definiteness and indefiniteness are determined by the intended usage built into the word's coinage, not by accidents of speech or listener perception. This aligns with the Hanafi emphasis on the authoritative nature of wad'.

Key Terms
معرفة (maʿrifa): A noun coined to be used for a specific (mu'ayyan) thing.
نكرة (nakira): A noun coined to be used for a non-specific thing (la bi-'aynihi).
الوضع (al-waḍʿ): The original coinage of a word for a particular type of referent (specific or non-specific).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred definition in this passage, what determines whether a noun is definite or indefinite?
A) Whether the listener knows the referent.
B) Whether the word is used in a loose (mutlaq) context.
C) The original coinage (wad') of the word for a specific or non-specific thing.
D) The presence or absence of the definite article 'al-'.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of ma'rifa and nakira rests on wad' (coinage) for specificity, ensuring objectivity in legal interpretation.

Definition and Classification of Ishtiqaq (Derivation) in Hanafi Usul
Commentary on derivation and noun classification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two definitions of ishtiqaq (knowledge and practice) and classify nouns as derived or non-derived.

Core Idea
Ishtiqaq is either the reallation found between two words or the act of creating a new word from original letters.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi understanding of derivation (ishtiqaq). The commentator clarifies the two perspectives: knowledge-based, where you recognize correspondence between words, and practice-based, where you take letters to form a new meaning. He also distinguishes proper nouns and genus nouns as either derived or not, with examples like Hatim (derived) and Zaid (non-derived). Understanding these classifications is foundational for analyzing general (amm) and specific (khass) texts in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions of ishtiqaq
Find in the passage the two ways derivation is defined: one from the perspective of knowledge (ilm) and one from practice (amal). Note the distinct wording.
2
Apply to examples
Use the examples given: Hatim (derived), Maqtal (derived), Zaid (non-derived), Rajul (non-derived). Determine why each is classified as such.
Proper noun 'Hatim' is derived from the root meaning 'strike' but is used as a name; its derivation is from the original meaning, not the proper meaning.
3
Distinguish proper noun vs. genus noun
The commentator says the proper noun is not derived in its proper meaning, only in its original meaning. Thus, the real derived noun is the genus noun (ism al-jins). Understand this distinction.
4
Link to usul discussions
Recognize that this classification matters when analyzing texts for amm and khass. For example, a derived noun like 'darib' (striker) is a sifat not a genus noun, so it's treated differently in usul.
This connects to the later discussion of mutlaq and muqayyad.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

عبارت میں ۔ ہذا ما تیسر لی من تقریر سید شریف فی جواب ہذا الاعتراض۔ ( قَوْلُهُ وَهُمَا أَى : الْعَلَمُ وَاسْمُ الْجِنسِ إِمَّا مُشْتَفَّانٍ كَحَاتِمٍ وَمَقْتَلٍ وَلَا يَصِحُ التَّمْثِيلُ بِنَحْوِ ضَارِبٍ ؛ لِأَنَّهُ جَعَلَ الصَّفَةَ قَسِيمًا لِاسْمِ الْجِنْسِ أَوْ لَا كَزَيْدٍ وَرَجُلٍ ، وَالاشْتِقَاقُ يُفَسِّرُ تَارَةً بِاعْتِبَارِ الْعِلْمِ فَيُقَالُ هُوَ أَنْ تَجِدَ بَيْنَ اللَّفْظَيُنِ وَعَدَمِهِ أَنْ يَكُونَ ذَلِكَ بِحَسَبِ دَلَالَةٍ اللَّفْظِ ، وَلَا عِبْرَةَ بِحَالَةِ الْإِطْلَاقِ دُونَ الْوَضْعِ وَلَا بِمَا عِندَ السَّامِعِ دُونَ الْمُتَكَلِّمِ عَلَى مَا ذَهَبَ إِلَيْهِ الْمُصَنِّفُ رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى ؛ لأنَّهُ إِذَا قَالَ جَاءَ نِي رَجُلٌ يُمْكِنُ أَنْ يَكُونَ الرَّجُلُ مُعَيَّنَا لِلسَّامِع أَيْضًا إِلَّا أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِحَسَبٍ دَلَالَةِ اللَّفْظِ . ( قَوْلُهُ وَاعْلَمُ أَنَّهُ يَجِبُ إِلَخُ ) يُرِيدُ أَنَّ تَمَايُزَ الْأَقْسَامِ الْمَذْكُورَةِ لَيْسَ بِحَسَبِ الدَّاتِ ، بَلْ بِحَسَبِ الْحَيْثِيَّاتِ وَالاعْتِبَارَاتِ وَالْحَيْثِيَّتَانِ قَدْ لَا تَتَنَافَيَانِ كَالوَضْعِ الْكَثِيرِ لِلْمَعْنَى الْكَثِيرِ وَوَضْعِ وَاحِدٍ لِافْرَادِ مَعْنِّى وَاحِدٍ كَمَا فِي لَفْظِ الْعُيُونِ فَإِنَّهُ عَامٌ مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهُ وُضِعَ وَضْعًا وَاحِدًا لِأَفْرَادِ الْعَيْنِ الْجَارِيَةِ ، وَمُشْتَرَكَ مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهُ وُضِعَ وَضُعًا كَثِيرًا لِلْعَيْنِ الْجَارِيَةِ ، وَالْعَيْنِ الْبَاصِرَةِ وَالشَّمْسِ وَالذَّهَبِ وَغَيْرِ ذَلِكَ ، وَقَدْ تَتَنَافَيَانِ كَالْوَضْعِ لِكَثِيرٍ غَيْرِ مَحْصُورٍ وَالْوَضْعِ لِوَاحِدٍ أَوْ لِكَثِيرٍ مَحْصُورٍ فَاللَّفْظُ الْوَاحِدُ لَا يَكُونُ عَامَّا وَخَاصًا بِاعْتِبَارِ الحَيْنِيَّتَيْنِ ؛ لأنَّ الْحَيْنِيَّتَيْنِ مُتَنَافِيَتَان لَا تَجْتَمِعَانِ فِي لَفْظِ وَاحِدٍ ، وَمَا ذُكِرَ مِنْ أَنَّ النَّكِرَةَ الْمَوْصُوفَةَ خَاصَّةٌ مِنْ وَجْهِ عَامَّةٌ مِنْ وَجْهِ فَسَيَجِيءُ جَوَابُهُ ، هَذَا غَايَهُ مَا تَكَلَّفَت لِتَقْرِيرِ هَذَا التَّقْسِيمِ وَتَبَيْنِ أَقْسَامِهِ ، وَالْكَلَامُ بَعْدُ مَوْضِعُ نَظَرٍ ترجمہ:۔ اس کا قول ہما یعنی علم اور اسم جنس یا تو دونوں مشتق ہوں گے جیسے حاتم اور مقتل ہے۔ اور نہیں ہے صحیح مثال بیان کرنا ضارب کی مثل کے ساتھ کیونکہ اس نے بنایا ہے صفت کو قسیم ( مقابل ) اسم جنس کا یا ( مشتق ) نہیں ہوں گے جیسے زید اور رجل ہے۔ اور اتفاق کی تفسیر کی جاتی ہے کبھی باعتبار علم کے پس کہا جاتا ہے کہ وہ یہ ہے کہ پائے تو دو لفظوں میں مناسبت اصل معنی اور ترکیب میں پس لوٹ جائے ان میں سے ایک دوسرے پر ۔ اور مرد و مشتق اور مردو دالیہ مشتق منہ ہے اور کبھی با عتبار عمل کے پس کہا جاتا ہے کہ وہ یہ ہے کہ لے تو لفظ میں سے وہ جو مناسب ہو اس کے حروف اصلیہ اور اس کی ترتیب میں پس بنا دے تو اس کو دلالت کرنے والا ایسے معنی پر کہ مناسب ہو اس کا معنی پس ماخوذ مشتق ہے اور ماخوذ منہ مشتق منہ ہے اور نہیں ہے مخفی یہ بات کہ بے شک علم نہیں ہوتا مشتق باعتبار معنی علمی کے بلکہ باعتبار معنی اصلی کے جو منقول عنہ ہے پس مشتق حقیقتا وہ اسم جنس ہے نہ اس کا ظہیر۔ قوله ان ارید منہ اسمی:۔ اس کا قول کہ اگر مراد لیا جائے اس سے مفہوم بغیر قید کے تو وہ مطلق ہے یہ شعر ہے اس بات کی
For the purpose of answering, one had to say the genus derived. The famous example is that a tree is produced from the seed; when the seed itself is sown wrongly, then crookedness continues in the tree. So the summary is that the objection is in your speech, not in the text of the author. This is what I could easily present from the speech of Sayyid Sharif in response to this objection. (His statement: 'And they both, i.e., the proper noun and the genus noun, are either derived like Hatim and Maqtal, and it is not correct to give an example like Darib, because he made the adjective a counterpart of the genus noun, or not derived like Zaid and Rajul. Derivation is interpreted sometimes from the perspective of knowledge: it is said that you find between two words a correspondence in the original meaning and composition, then you refer one to the other; the referred is derived and the referred-to is derived from it. And sometimes from the perspective of practice: it is said that you take from the word what is suitable to it in its original letters and their order, then you make it indicative of a meaning that suits its meaning; the taken is derived and the taken from is derived from it. It is not hidden that the proper noun is not derived from the perspective of the proper meaning, but from the perspective of the original meaning from which it is transferred. So the derived in reality is the genus noun and nothing else. His statement: 'If what is meant by it is the named without a restriction, then it is absolute' indicates that the intended in the absolute is the named itself, not the individual. It is not so, because it is certain that the intended in His statement 'then the freeing of a believing neck' is the freeing of an individual from the individuals of this concept, unrestricted by any accidents. His statement: 'For it is what was placed' — since what emerges from the division is some types of the indefinite, which is what is used for the individual not the named itself, and in contrast to it are some sections of the definite, which is the mentally known — he presented the definitions of the definite and indefinite in a way that encompasses all the sections. His statement: 'upon unrestrictedness and for the listener' are two restrictions for specification and non-specification. Better in their definition is what was said: the definite is what was placed to be used for a thing itself, and the indefinite is what was placed to be used for a thing not itself. So the consideration in specification and non-specification is that it be according to the indication of the word, and no attention is given to the state of unrestrictedness without placement, nor to what is with the listener without the speaker, according to what the author may Allah have mercy on him held, because if he says 'a man came to me,' it is possible that the man is specified for the listener too, except that it is not according to the indication of the word. (His statement: 'And know that it is obligatory etc.') He intends that the distinction of the mentioned sections is not by essence, but by aspects and considerations. The two aspects may not be mutually exclusive, like the multiple placements for multiple meanings and one placement for individuals of one meaning, as in the word 'al-uyun,' for it is general in that it was placed with one placement for the individuals of the flowing eye, and it is homonymous in that it was placed with multiple placements for the flowing eye, the seeing eye, the sun, gold, and others. And they may be mutually exclusive, like placement for many unlimited and placement for one or for many limited. So the single word cannot be both general and specific by the two perspectives, because the two perspectives are mutually exclusive and cannot come together in one word. What was mentioned that the described indefinite is specific in one way and general in another will have its answer given later. This is the utmost of what I undertook to establish this division and explain its sections, and the speech after this is a place of scrutiny. Translation: His statement 'huma' i.e., the proper noun and the genus noun are either both derived, like Hatim and Maqtal. And it is not correct to give an example like Darib, because he made the adjective a counterpart of the genus noun, or they are not derived, like Zaid and Rajul. And derivation is interpreted sometimes from the perspective of knowledge: it is said that you find between two words correspondence in the original meaning and composition, then you refer one to the other; the referred is derived and the referred-to is derived from it. And sometimes from the perspective of practice: it is said that you take from the word what is suitable to it in its original letters and their order, then you make it indicative of a meaning that suits its meaning; the taken is derived and the taken from is derived from it. And it is not hidden that the proper noun is not derived from the perspective of the proper meaning, but from the perspective of the original meaning from which it is transferred. So the derived in reality is the genus noun and nothing else. His statement: 'If what is meant by it is the named without a restriction, then it is absolute' indicates...
Source pages p. 528

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the classification of nouns and the definitions of definite (ma'rifah) and indefinite (nakirah) in Hanafi usul. The author begins by discussing a previous objection regarding the use of 'derived genus' (jins mushtaq). He provides an analogy: if the seed is sown wrongly, the tree will be crooked, implying the objection is in the opponent's reasoning, not the text. Then he moves to the core: proper nouns ('alam) and genus nouns (ism al-jins) are either derived (e.g., Hatim, Maqtal) or not (e.g., Zaid, Rajul). He clarifies that derivation (ishtiqaq) has two definitions: one based on knowledge (finding correspondence and referring one word to another), and one based on usage (taking letters and forming a new word). A proper noun is not derived in its proper meaning but in its original transferred meaning. The commentator then critiques the definition of 'mutlaq' (absolute) given in the matn: the absolute means the named itself, not the individual. He provides Quranic evidence (4:92: 'freeing a believing neck') to show that the absolute refers to an individual unrestricted by accidents. Next, he addresses the definitions of ma'rifah and nakirah. The matn's definition (based on usage upon unrestrictedness and the listener) is criticized. A better definition is: the definite is placed for a specific thing, the indefinite for a non-specific thing, based on the word's indication, not the speaker's context or the listener's knowledge. The author then explains that the divisions of nouns are not by essence but by aspects (haythiyyat). For example, 'al-uyun' is general (from one placement for many individuals of the same kind) and homonymous (from multiple placements for different meanings) simultaneously. But general and specific cannot coexist in one word from the same perspective. The passage ends with the commentator noting that further discussion on the described indefinite being specific in one way and general in another will be addressed later, and that the speech is still under scrutiny. This commentary is a critical refinement of the matn's definitions, showing the Hanafi approach to precision in usul terminology.

Key Terms
اشْتِقَاق (ishtiqāq): Derivation: either finding correspondence in meaning and composition and referring one word to another (knowledge perspective), or taking letters suitable to the original and making it indicative of a related meaning (practice perspective).
مُطْلَق (muṭlaq): The absolute: that which indicates the named itself without any restriction, as opposed to the individual.
الْمَعْرِفَة (al-maʿrifah): The definite: what is placed (by wad') to be used for a specific thing (shay' bi-'aynihi).
النَّكِرَة (al-nakirah): The indefinite: what is placed to be used for a non-specific thing (shay' la bi-'aynihi).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a correct example of a derived proper noun?
A) Zaid
B) Rajul
C) Hatim
D) Darib

Key Takeaway
Derivation in Hanafi usul is defined both by recognition of correspondence and by active formation, with proper nouns being derived only in their original meaning.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
6 min
Definite (Ma'rifah) and Indefinite (Nakirah): The Hanafi Definition and Dispute
Critique of definitions of definite/indefinite and discussion of mutlaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi definition of ma'rifah and nakirah based on wad' (linguistic placement) and critique the alternative definition involving unrestrictedness and listener's knowledge.

Core Idea
The Hanafi definition: ma'rifah is what is placed for a specific thing; nakirah is what is placed for a non-specific thing, regardless of context.

This lesson focuses on the correct definitions of definite (ma'rifah) and indefinite (nakirah) in Hanafi usul. The commentator criticizes the author's definition that includes 'upon unrestrictedness' and 'for the listener,' arguing that the specification should be based solely on the word's indication (dalalah lafz). He provides the example 'ja'a ni rajul' to show that a word can be specific to the listener but still be indefinite in linguistic form. Understanding these definitions is crucial for interpreting mutlaq and muqayyad texts in the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definitions of ma'rifah and nakirah in the passage
Find the two definitions: the one from the matn (with qaydayn: 'ala al-itlaq wa li al-sami') and the one the commentator prefers (based on wad').
2
Identify the points of critique
The commentator says the matn's definitions include two restrictions: 'upon unrestrictedness' and 'for the listener.' He argues that these are irrelevant because the specification should be by the word's indication, not the context.
He gives the example of 'ja'a ni rajul' where the listener may know the man, but the word itself is indefinite.
3
Understand the preferred definition
The commentator's preferred definition: ma'rifah is what is placed to be used for a specific thing (shay' bi-'aynihi), and nakirah is what is placed for a non-specific thing. The key is the word's placement (wad'), not the state of utterance.
This aligns with the Hanafi emphasis on linguistic analysis over contextual factors.
4
Connect to mutlaq/muqayyad discussion
Earlier in the passage, the commentator discusses the meaning of mutlaq in the Quranic verse 'tahriru raqabatin mu'minatin.' He argues that mutlaq refers to an individual unrestricted by accidents, not the abstract essence. This connects to the definition of nakirah because nakirah is also unrestricted.
5
Apply the definitions to a fiqh example
Consider the word 'raqabah' in the Quran: is it mutlaq or muqayyad? According to the Hanafi definition of nakirah, it indicates an unrestricted individual, but the description 'mu'minatin' makes it muqayyad. Understanding nakirah helps in this analysis.

الْخَارِجُ مِنَ التَّقْسِيمِ بَعْضَ أَنْوَاعِ النَّكِرَةِ وَهُوَ مَا اسْتُعْمِلَ فِي الْفَرْدِ دُونَ نَفْسِ الْمُسَمَّى وَفِي مُقَابَلَتِهِ بَعْضُ أَقْسَامِ الْمَعْرِفَةِ وَهُوَ الْمَعْهُودُ الْحَيْنِيَّتَيْنِ مُتَنَافِيَتَان لَا تَجْتَمِعَانِ فِي لَفْظِ وَاحِدٍ ، وَمَا ذُكِرَ مِنْ أَنَّ النَّكِرَةَ الْمَوْصُوفَةَ خَاصَّةٌ مِنْ وَجْهِ عَامَّةٌ مِنْ وَجْهِ فَسَيَجِيءُ جَوَابُهُ ، هَذَا غَايَهُ مَا تَكَلَّفَت لِتَقْرِيرِ هَذَا التَّقْسِيمِ وَتَبَيْنِ أَقْسَامِهِ ، وَالْكَلَامُ بَعْدُ مَوْضِعُ نَظَرٍ ترجمہ:۔ اس کا قول ہما یعنی علم اور اسم جنس یا تو دونوں مشتق ہوں گے جیسے حاتم اور مقتل ہے۔ اور نہیں ہے صحیح مثال بیان کرنا ضارب کی مثل کے ساتھ کیونکہ اس نے بنایا ہے صفت کو قسیم ( مقابل ) اسم جنس کا یا ( مشتق ) نہیں ہوں گے جیسے زید اور رجل ہے۔ اور اتفاق کی تفسیر کی جاتی ہے کبھی باعتبار علم کے پس کہا جاتا ہے کہ وہ یہ ہے کہ پائے تو دو لفظوں میں مناسبت اصل معنی اور ترکیب میں پس لوٹ جائے ان میں سے ایک دوسرے پر ۔ اور مرد و مشتق اور مردو دالیہ مشتق منہ ہے اور کبھی با عتبار عمل کے پس کہا جاتا ہے کہ وہ یہ ہے کہ لے تو لفظ میں سے وہ جو مناسب ہو اس کے حروف اصلیہ اور اس کی ترتیب میں پس بنا دے تو اس کو دلالت کرنے والا ایسے معنی پر کہ مناسب ہو اس کا معنی پس ماخوذ مشتق ہے اور ماخوذ منہ مشتق منہ ہے اور نہیں ہے مخفی یہ بات کہ بے شک علم نہیں ہوتا مشتق باعتبار معنی علمی کے بلکہ باعتبار معنی اصلی کے جو منقول عنہ ہے پس مشتق حقیقتا وہ اسم جنس ہے نہ اس کا ظہیر۔ قوله ان ارید منہ اسمی:۔ اس کا قول کہ اگر مراد لیا جائے اس سے مفہوم بغیر قید کے تو وہ مطلق ہے یہ شعر ہے اس بات کی
For the purpose of answering, one had to say the genus derived. The famous example is that a tree is produced from the seed; when the seed itself is sown wrongly, then crookedness continues in the tree. So the summary is that the objection is in your speech, not in the text of the author. This is what I could easily present from the speech of Sayyid Sharif in response to this objection. (His statement: 'And they both, i.e., the proper noun and the genus noun, are either derived like Hatim and Maqtal, and it is not correct to give an example like Darib, because he made the adjective a counterpart of the genus noun, or not derived like Zaid and Rajul. Derivation is interpreted sometimes from the perspective of knowledge: it is said that you find between two words a correspondence in the original meaning and composition, then you refer one to the other; the referred is derived and the referred-to is derived from it. And sometimes from the perspective of practice: it is said that you take from the word what is suitable to it in its original letters and their order, then you make it indicative of a meaning that suits its meaning; the taken is derived and the taken from is derived from it. It is not hidden that the proper noun is not derived from the perspective of the proper meaning, but from the perspective of the original meaning from which it is transferred. So the derived in reality is the genus noun and nothing else. His statement: 'If what is meant by it is the named without a restriction, then it is absolute' indicates that the intended in the absolute is the named itself, not the individual. It is not so, because it is certain that the intended in His statement 'then the freeing of a believing neck' is the freeing of an individual from the individuals of this concept, unrestricted by any accidents. His statement: 'For it is what was placed' — since what emerges from the division is some types of the indefinite, which is what is used for the individual not the named itself, and in contrast to it are some sections of the definite, which is the mentally known — he presented the definitions of the definite and indefinite in a way that encompasses all the sections. His statement: 'upon unrestrictedness and for the listener' are two restrictions for specification and non-specification. Better in their definition is what was said: the definite is what was placed to be used for a thing itself, and the indefinite is what was placed to be used for a thing not itself. So the consideration in specification and non-specification is that it be according to the indication of the word, and no attention is given to the state of unrestrictedness without placement, nor to what is with the listener without the speaker, according to what the author may Allah have mercy on him held, because if he says 'a man came to me,' it is possible that the man is specified for the listener too, except that it is not according to the indication of the word. (His statement: 'And know that it is obligatory etc.') He intends that the distinction of the mentioned sections is not by essence, but by aspects and considerations. The two aspects may not be mutually exclusive, like the multiple placements for multiple meanings and one placement for individuals of one meaning, as in the word 'al-uyun,' for it is general in that it was placed with one placement for the individuals of the flowing eye, and it is homonymous in that it was placed with multiple placements for the flowing eye, the seeing eye, the sun, gold, and others. And they may be mutually exclusive, like placement for many unlimited and placement for one or for many limited. So the single word cannot be both general and specific by the two perspectives, because the two perspectives are mutually exclusive and cannot come together in one word. What was mentioned that the described indefinite is specific in one way and general in another will have its answer given later. This is the utmost of what I undertook to establish this division and explain its sections, and the speech after this is a place of scrutiny. Translation: His statement 'huma' i.e., the proper noun and the genus noun are either both derived, like Hatim and Maqtal. And it is not correct to give an example like Darib, because he made the adjective a counterpart of the genus noun, or they are not derived, like Zaid and Rajul. And derivation is interpreted sometimes from the perspective of knowledge: it is said that you find between two words correspondence in the original meaning and composition, then you refer one to the other; the referred is derived and the referred-to is derived from it. And sometimes from the perspective of practice: it is said that you take from the word what is suitable to it in its original letters and their order, then you make it indicative of a meaning that suits its meaning; the taken is derived and the taken from is derived from it. And it is not hidden that the proper noun is not derived from the perspective of the proper meaning, but from the perspective of the original meaning from which it is transferred. Source pages p. 528

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the classification of nouns and the definitions of definite (ma'rifah) and indefinite (nakirah) in Hanafi usul. The author begins by discussing a previous objection regarding the use of 'derived genus' (jins mushtaq). He provides an analogy: if the seed is sown wrongly, the tree will be crooked, implying the objection is in the opponent's reasoning, not the text. Then he moves to the core: proper nouns ('alam) and genus nouns (ism al-jins) are either derived (e.g., Hatim, Maqtal) or not (e.g., Zaid, Rajul). He clarifies that derivation (ishtiqaq) has two definitions: one based on knowledge (finding correspondence and referring one word to another), and one based on usage (taking letters and forming a new word). A proper noun is not derived in its proper meaning but in its original transferred meaning. The commentator then critiques the definition of 'mutlaq' (absolute) given in the matn: the absolute means the named itself, not the individual. He provides Quranic evidence (4:92: 'freeing a believing neck') to show that the absolute refers to an individual unrestricted by accidents. Next, he addresses the definitions of ma'rifah and nakirah. The matn's definition (based on usage upon unrestrictedness and the listener) is criticized. A better definition is: the definite is placed for a specific thing, the indefinite for a non-specific thing, based on the word's indication, not the speaker's context or the listener's knowledge. The author then explains that the divisions of nouns are not by essence but by aspects (haythiyyat). For example, 'al-uyun' is general (from one placement for many individuals of the same kind) and homonymous (from multiple placements for different meanings) simultaneously. But general and specific cannot coexist in one word from the same perspective. The passage ends with the commentator noting that further discussion on the described indefinite being specific in one way and general in another will be addressed later, and that the speech is still under scrutiny. This commentary is a critical refinement of the matn's definitions, showing the Hanafi approach to precision in usul terminology.

Key Terms
اشْتِقَاق (ishtiqāq): Derivation: either finding correspondence in meaning and composition and referring one word to another (knowledge perspective), or taking letters suitable to the original and making it indicative of a related meaning (practice perspective).
مُطْلَق (muṭlaq): The absolute: that which indicates the named itself without any restriction, as opposed to the individual.
الْمَعْرِفَة (al-maʿrifah): The definite: what is placed (by wad') to be used for a specific thing (shay' bi-'aynihi).
النَّكِرَة (al-nakirah): The indefinite: what is placed to be used for a non-specific thing (shay' la bi-'aynihi).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the correct basis for defining a noun as ma'rifah or nakirah?
A) The speaker's intention
B) The listener's knowledge
C) The word's linguistic placement (wad')
D) The presence of the definite article 'al-'

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, definiteness is a property of the word's placement, not context; thus ma'rifah is what is placed for a specific thing, and nakirah for a non-specific thing.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
8 min
Can a Word be Both General and Specific? The Role of Aspects (Haythiyyat) in Hanafi Usul
Explaining that a word can be general and specific from different considerations, with the example of 'al-uyun'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the different aspects (haythiyyat) that make a word general (amm) and homonymous (mushtarak), and explain why a single aspect cannot yield both general and specific status.

Core Idea
A word can be both general and specific only if considered from different aspects; if the aspects conflict, it cannot be both.

In Hanafi usul, words are classified as general (amm), specific (khass), homonymous (mushtarak), etc. But a single word might appear to fit multiple categories. The commentator in this passage resolves this by appealing to aspects (haythiyyat): a word can be general from one perspective and something else from another. The example of 'al-uyun' (the eyes/springs) illustrates this. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly deriving rulings from scriptural texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core question
The passage addresses: can a single word be both general and specific? The answer depends on the aspect (haythiyya) under consideration.
Aspects are analytical perspectives, not inherent properties of the word.
2
Analyze the example of 'al-uyun'
The word 'al-uyun' is general (amm) because it is placed with one wad' for all individuals of 'flowing eye' (springs). It is also homonymous (mushtarak) because it has multiple wad's for different meanings (spring, seeing eye, sun, gold, etc.).
Here, 'general' refers to coverage of individuals of a single meaning; 'homonymous' refers to multiple distinct meanings.
3
Understand when aspects conflict
If a word is placed for many unlimited individuals (general) and simultaneously for a single individual (specific) or a limited set, the aspects conflict, and the word cannot be both general and specific.
Conflict occurs when the wad' themselves are contradictory, not just different perspectives.
4
Apply to the definition of definite and indefinite
The passage also clarifies that the definiteness of a word (ma'rifa vs nakira) depends on its linguistic indication (dalala al-lafz), not on whether the speaker or listener knows the referent.
Example: 'a man came' (ja'a ni rajul) – the word 'rajul' is indefinite even if the listener knows the man, because the word's placement is for non-specific referents.
5
Note the pending issue
The commentator mentions that the claim that a qualified indefinite is both specific and general from different aspects will be addressed later, implying a further refinement.
This shows the critical refinement process ongoing in the text.

کلام اس ( تفصیل ) کے بعد بھی محل نظر ہے۔ توضیح متن تشریح: قولہ و ہما:۔ یہ شرح کی عبارت کا پہلا حصہ ہے۔ اس میں شارخ نے متن کی وضاحت کی ہے صاحب توضیح نے علم
According to what the author, may Allah have mercy on him, held; because when he says 'a man came to me,' it is possible that the man is also specified for the listener, except that it is not by virtue of the indication of the word. (His statement: 'And know that it is necessary, etc.') He intends that the distinction of the mentioned categories is not by virtue of essence, but by virtue of aspects and considerations. And the two aspects may not conflict, like the multiple positions for multiple meanings and one position for individuals of one meaning, as in the word 'al-uyun', for it is general by virtue that it was placed with one position for individuals of the 'flowing eye', and it is homonymous by virtue that it was placed with multiple positions for the flowing eye, the seeing eye, the sun, gold, and others. And they may conflict, like the position for many unlimited and the position for one or for many limited; so a single word cannot be general and specific by virtue of the two aspects, because the two aspects are conflicting and do not come together in one word. And what was mentioned that the qualified indefinite is specific in one aspect and general in another, its answer will come later. This is the utmost that I have exerted myself to clarify this division and explain its categories, and the speech after this is a place of scrutiny. Translation: His statement 'huma' meaning that both 'ilm and ism jins are either both derived like Hatim and Maqtal, and it is not correct to give the example like 'Darib' because he made the adjective a partner (opposite) of ism jins, or they are not derived like Zayd and Rijal. And agreement is interpreted sometimes by virtue of knowledge, so it is said that it is that you find in two words a suitability in original meaning and composition, so one of them refers to the other. And 'murd' and 'murd'? and 'murd aliyya' is derived from it. And sometimes the two aspects conflict, like the position for many unlimited and the position for one or many limited, so a single word cannot be general and specific by virtue of the two aspects, because the two aspects are conflicting, they do not come together in one word. And what was mentioned that the qualified indefinite is specific in one aspect and general in another, its answer will come soon. This is the utmost that I have exerted myself to clarify this division and explain its categories, and the speech after this (detail) is still a place of scrutiny. Tawdih text Commentary: His statement 'huma': This is the first part of the commentary's expression. In it, the commentator explains the text. The author of al-Tawdih said 'ilm...
Source pages p. 529

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis from the Hanafi usul work Al-Tawdih with its commentary. The commentator (likely Taftazani) examines the classification of words into general (amm) and specific (khass), and definite (ma'rifa) and indefinite (nakira). The core argument is that a single word can be both general and specific from different aspects (haythiyyat), but not if those aspects are mutually exclusive. The example given is 'al-uyun' (the eyes/springs): it is general when considering its placement (wad') for multiple individuals of 'flowing eye' (al-ayn al-jariya), but it is homonymous (mushtarak) when considering its multiple placements for different meanings like flowing eye, seeing eye, sun, gold, etc. This shows that a word can have two different statuses from different perspectives. However, if the aspect is that the word is placed for many unlimited (like 'amm') and for one individual (like 'khass'), these aspects conflict, so the same word cannot simultaneously be general and specific in that sense. The commentator also addresses the definition of definite and indefinite. He clarifies that the criterion for definiteness is the word's indication (dalala al-lafz), not the speaker's or listener's knowledge. For example, saying 'a man came' (ja'a ni rajul) may have a specified man in the listener's mind, but the word 'rajul' is still indefinite because its linguistic indication is non-specific. The commentator ends by expressing that his explanation of this division is thorough, but the matter remains open for scrutiny, indicating a critical refinement of the original text. The Urdu text then continues with further elaboration on derived and non-derived nouns, but the commentary primarily focuses on the conceptual distinctions.

Key Terms
عام (amm): A word that by its placement (wad') indicates all individuals of its meaning without limitation.
خاص (khass): A word that indicates a specific individual or a limited set of individuals.
مشترك (mushtarak): A word placed for multiple meanings with multiple placements (wad'). It is homonymous.
نكرة (nakira): A word put to be used for a non-specific (non-definite) referent.
معرفة (ma'rifa): A word put to be used for a specific (definite) referent.
حيثية (haythiyya): An aspect or consideration under which a word is analyzed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can the word 'al-uyun' be considered general (amm) from one aspect and homonymous (mushtarak) from another?
A) Because it is derived from a root that has both general and specific connotations.
B) Because it has one wad' for individuals of 'flowing eye' (general) and multiple wad's for different meanings (homonymous).
C) Because the listener can interpret it either way.
D) Because the speaker intends it to be both general and specific simultaneously.

Key Takeaway
A word's classification as general or specific depends on the aspect (haythiyya) considered; different aspects can yield different and even complementary statuses, as illustrated by 'al-uyun' being both general and homonymous.

Defining Ma'rifah and Nakirah: Wad' over Context
Critique of alternative definitions and affirmation of wad'-based classification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to APPLY the Hanafi definition of ma'rifah and nakirah to classify words as 'aam or khaas, and EVALUATE why the definition is based on wad' rather than context.

Core Idea
Definiteness is determined by the word's linguistic placement, not speaker or listener context.

This passage from the commentary refines the definitions of ma'rifah (definite) and nakirah (indefinite), emphasizing that the key is the word's fixed placement (wad') for specific or non-specific meaning. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly identifying whether a text is 'aam (general) or khaas (specific), and it guards against subjective interpretations that could undermine legal consistency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions being critiqued
The text mentions definitions that rely on the state of utterance ('inda iṭlāq) and the listener's knowledge. Recognize these as alternative views.
The commentator explicitly rejects these.
2
Understand the Hanafi preferred definition
The commentator states: 'the definite is that which is placed (wuḍiʿa) to be used for a specific thing, and the indefinite for a non-specific thing.' Note that the basis is wad'.
This is the core Hanafi position.
3
Analyze the example of 'a man'
The text gives 'jā'a rajul' (a man came). Even if the listener knows the man, the word remains indefinite because its wad' is for non-specific indication. This illustrates the rule.
Context does not change the linguistic classification.
4
Distinguish between 'aam and khaas with the example of 'uyun
The word 'uyun (eyes/springs) is 'aam in one aspect (placed with a single placement for flowing spring individuals) and mushtarak (homonymous) in another (multiple placements). Two contradictory aspects cannot combine, so it cannot be both 'aam and khaas.
Aspects can be non-contradictory or contradictory; contradictory ones prevent a word from being both general and specific simultaneously.

Commentary

The author discusses that the correct definition is based on the word's indication of specification or non-specification, not on the listener's context. He gives the example of 'uyun which can be both 'aam and mushtarak depending on the aspect considered. The commentator concludes that his exposition is thorough but still open to scrutiny.

Key Terms
المعرفۃ (al-ma'rifah): What is placed to be used for a specific meaning.
النکرۃ (al-nakirah): What is placed to be used for a non-specific meaning.
وضع (wad'): The linguistic convention that assigns a meaning to a word.

Two Definitions of Ishtiqaq: Theoretical vs Practical Derivation
Defining two types of linguistic derivation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two definitions of ishtiqaq (theoretical and practical) and explain the difference between them using the criteria of meaning correspondence vs letter correspondence.

Core Idea
Ishtiqaq requires correspondence in both letters and meaning; theoretical derivation focuses on meaning, practical on form.

This passage introduces the concept of ishtiqaq (derivation) as understood in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The commentator presents two distinct definitions: one theoretical, based on conceptual correspondence, and one practical, based on formal letter correspondence. Understanding these definitions is crucial for later discussions on how words convey meanings and affect legal interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Locate the theoretical and practical definitions of ishtiqaq. Theoretical derivation considers correspondence in original meaning and letter arrangement at the conceptual level. Practical derivation considers only correspondence in original letters and arrangement.
2
Break down the components
For each definition, list the required elements: theoretical requires meaning correspondence; practical requires only letter correspondence.
The difference is subtle but important.
3
Compare and contrast
The key difference: theoretical derivation requires that the derived word's meaning corresponds to the root meaning; practical derivation does not.
Theoretical derivation is more comprehensive.
4
Apply to an example
Apply both definitions to 'dharaba' → 'dharib'. Under theoretical, it is derived because the meaning of 'one who hits' relates to the act of hitting. Under practical, it is also derived because letters correspond.
The commentator later notes that 'dharib' is an adjective, not a common noun.
5
Understand the usul relevance
In usul al-fiqh, derivative words carry implied relational meanings that affect rulings (e.g., al-amr yufidu al-wujub relies on the verb being derived). Non-derived words are simple labels.
This sets the stage for the next lesson on proper nouns.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

From here the commentator relates a benefit: that dharib cannot be made an example of a derived common noun because dharib is a form of adjective, and the author made the adjective a type of common noun. Now if we make it an example of a common noun, the combination of two parts would be necessary, and that is invalid. Definitions of derivation: 'And derivation is explained' – this is the third part of the commentary's expression. From here the commentator explains the meaning of derivation. Derivation has two definitions: theoretical and practical. Theoretical definition: it is that theoretical derivation is said to be a correspondence between two words in terms of the original meaning and the arrangement of letters such that in the mind, at the level of conception, one word is returned to another. What is returned is the derived term, and what it is returned to is the source term. Practical definition: practical derivation is said to be that one word is taken from another such that there is a correspondence in its original letters and their arrangement, then that word is made indicative of a meaning that has correspondence with the meaning of what it is derived from. For example, from the word dharaba the word dharib is taken at the intellectual level, then it is considered that the original letters and arrangement of dharib correspond to dharab. The difference between them is that in theoretical derivation, correspondence between the two words in original meaning and composition is considered; in practical derivation, correspondence between the two words in original letters and composition is considered. 'Can a proper noun be derived? And not...' – this is the fourth part of the commentary's expression. From here the commentator objects to the author. Objection: the author said that common nouns and proper nouns have two types: derived and non-derived. This statement is wrong. Because when a proper noun is derived, it would indicate a specific entity, not the masdar meaning. For example, the word Hatim indicates a specific entity, not the masdar meaning. So saying that a proper noun is derived...
Source pages p. 531

Commentary
This passage covers the definitions of ishtiqaq (derivation) and an objection to categorizing proper nouns as derived. The commentator first explains the types of proper and common nouns given by the author of al-Tawdih, noting that derived and non-derived examples exist. He then clarifies that the word 'dharib' cannot serve as an example of a derived common noun because it is an adjective, and adjectives are a separate category. Next, he provides two definitions of ishtiqaq: theoretical (focusing on meaning correspondence) and practical (focusing on letter correspondence). Finally, he critiques the author's claim that proper nouns can be derived, arguing that derived proper nouns like Hatim indicate a specific entity rather than a secondary meaning derived from a root, so they cannot be considered derived in the same linguistic sense.

Key Terms
الاشتقاق (al-ishtiqaq): A linguistic relation between two words sharing root letters and meaning, either in concept (theoretical) or in form (practical).
المشتق (al-mushtaq): The derived word that is taken from another (like dharib from dharaba).
المشتق منہ (al-mushtaq minhu): The source word from which derivation occurs (like dharaba).
العلم (al-'alam): A proper noun indicating a specific entity, e.g., Zayd, Hatim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes theoretical derivation from practical derivation?
A) Theoretical derivation considers meaning correspondence; practical derivation considers only letter correspondence.
B) Theoretical derivation considers only letter correspondence; practical derivation considers meaning correspondence.
C) Both consider meaning and letters equally.
D) Neither considers meaning.

Key Takeaway
Ishtiqaq has two levels: the deeper theoretical level (meaning-correspondence) and the surface practical level (letter-correspondence); only theoretical derivation captures full linguistic relationship.

Can Proper Nouns Be Derived? A Hanafi Critique
Critiquing the derivation of proper nouns

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi commentator rejects the idea that proper nouns like 'Hatim' are derived, and apply the criterion of meaning indication to other examples.

Core Idea
A proper noun cannot be derived because it indicates a specific entity, not the meaning of its root.

The author of al-Tawdih claimed that proper nouns could be derived, offering examples like Hatim (from hatm). The commentator objects, arguing that derivation requires meaning correspondence, not just morphological form. This lesson examines the commentator's reasoning and its implications for usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
State the claim
The author of al-Tawdih claimed that proper nouns like Hatim are derived (mushtaq), i.e., taken from a root verb like hatm (to break).
This claim is based on the word's morphological pattern (fa'il).
2
Identify the commentator's objection
The commentator argues that this is wrong because a proper noun indicates a specific entity (dhat), not the masdar meaning (action).
Derivation requires that the word convey a meaning related to its root.
3
Analyze the example: Hatim
The word 'Hatim' is a person's name; it does not mean 'breaker' despite having the form fa'il and sharing letters with hatm.
If someone is known as a breaker, they might be called Hatim metaphorically, but as a proper noun it becomes a label.
4
Apply the criterion
According to the theoretical definition of ishtiqaq, derivation requires both letter and meaning correspondence. Hatim fails the meaning correspondence test.
This shows the importance of the theoretical over the practical definition in determining true derivation.
5
Consider the thamarah al-khilaf
In usul al-fiqh, derived words carry relational implications (e.g., al-dharib implies an act of hitting). Proper nouns are simple labels with no such implication, affecting how they are interpreted in legal texts.
If a proper noun were considered derived, it could carry additional semantic weight, altering the ruling.

Explanation: His statement 'and both of them' – this is the first part of the commentary's expression. In it, the commentator clarified the text. The author of al-Tawdih said about proper nouns and common nouns that sometimes they will be derived and sometimes non-derived. The commentator gives their examples: that a derived proper noun like Hatim, a derived common noun like maqtal, a non-derived proper noun like Zayd, and a non-derived common noun like rajul. 'And it is not correct' – this is the second part of the commentary's expression. From here the commentator relates a benefit: that dharib cannot be made an example of a derived common noun because dharib is a form of adjective, and the author made the adjective a type of common noun. Now if we make it an example of a common noun, the combination of two parts would be necessary, and that is invalid. Definitions of derivation: 'And derivation is explained' – this is the third part of the commentary's expression. From here the commentator explains the meaning of derivation. Derivation has two definitions: theoretical and practical. Theoretical definition: it is that theoretical derivation is said to be a correspondence between two words in terms of the original meaning and the arrangement of letters such that in the mind, at the level of conception, one word is returned to another. What is returned is the derived term, and what it is returned to is the source term. Practical definition: practical derivation is said to be that one word is taken from another such that there is a correspondence in its original letters and their arrangement, then that word is made indicative of a meaning that has correspondence with the meaning of what it is derived from. For example, from the word dharaba the word dharib is taken at the intellectual level, then it is considered that the original letters and arrangement of dharib correspond to dharab. The difference between them is that in theoretical derivation, correspondence between the two words in original meaning and composition is considered; in practical derivation, correspondence between the two words in original letters and composition is considered. 'Can a proper noun be derived? And not...' – this is the fourth part of the commentary's expression. From here the commentator objects to the author. Objection: the author said that common nouns and proper nouns have two types: derived and non-derived. This statement is wrong. Because when a proper noun is derived, it would indicate a specific entity, not the masdar meaning. For example, the word Hatim indicates a specific entity, not the masdar meaning. So saying that a proper noun is derived...
Source pages p. 531

Commentary
This passage covers the definitions of ishtiqaq (derivation) and an objection to categorizing proper nouns as derived. The commentator first explains the types of proper and common nouns given by the author of al-Tawdih, noting that derived and non-derived examples exist. He then clarifies that the word 'dharib' cannot serve as an example of a derived common noun because it is an adjective, and adjectives are a separate category. Next, he provides two definitions of ishtiqaq: theoretical (focusing on meaning correspondence) and practical (focusing on letter correspondence). Finally, he critiques the author's claim that proper nouns can be derived, arguing that derived proper nouns like Hatim indicate a specific entity rather than a secondary meaning derived from a root, so they cannot be considered derived in the same linguistic sense.

Key Terms
الاشتقاق (al-ishtiqaq): A linguistic relation between two words sharing root letters and meaning, either in concept (theoretical) or in form (practical).
المشتق (al-mushtaq): The derived word that is taken from another (like dharib from dharaba).
المشتق منہ (al-mushtaq minhu): The source word from which derivation occurs (like dharaba).
العلم (al-'alam): A proper noun indicating a specific entity, e.g., Zayd, Hatim.

Key Takeaway
A word's derived status is determined by meaning indication, not just morphological form; proper nouns are therefore non-derived.

The Hanafi Expansion of Ma'rifah and Nakirah Definitions Using 'Shay''
Explanation of why definitions use 'shay' to include meaning and individual

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Hanafi usul defines ma'rifah and nakirah with the word 'shay'' and identify which subtypes are thereby included.

Core Idea
To cover all subtypes, Hanafi definitions use 'shay''—including both meaning and individual.

In this passage, the commentator explains why the author of al-Tawdih defines ma'rifah and nakirah using the word 'shay''. The definitions derived from the basis of partition (wajh al-hasr) only cover some subtypes, leaving out those indicating meanings. By using 'shay'', the definitions become comprehensive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Problem
Recognize that the definitions from wajh al-hasr only cover nakirah indicating individuals, not meanings. The commentator gives the example of a nakirah that indicates a meaning (mafhum).
The phrase 'هذا نمرة الذي هو دال على الفرد' appears in the Arabic as an example.
2
Locate the Solution
The author defines nakirah as 'ma wudi'a li shay'in la bi-'aynihi' and ma'rifah as 'ma wudi'a li shay'in bi-'aynihi'. These definitions use 'shay'' to be general.
The definitions are explicitly quoted in the passage.
3
Analyze the Key Term 'Shay''
'Shay'' is a general term that covers both meaning (mafhum) and individual (fard). This ensures the definitions are not restricted to physical objects.
The commentator states: 'شئ عام، مفہوم ہو یا فرد ہو'.
4
Confirm the Coverage
With these definitions, all eight subtypes from the second division are included. The word 'shay'' removes the limitation of the previous definitions.
The passage mentions that the need arises for a definition 'شامل لجميع اقسام'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قولہ فہمی ماوضع :۔ اس عبارت سے شارح نے سوال مقدر کا جواب دیا ہے۔ سوال : تقسیم ثانی سے برآمد شدہ اقسام تو آٹھ ہیں تو مصنف کو کیا دلچسپی ہے معرفہ اور نکرہ کے ساتھ کہ مائن نے تعریف صرف انہی دو کی ہے باقی اقسام کی تعریفات بیان نہیں فرمائیں۔ جواب:۔ اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ وجہ حصر سے معرفہ اور نکرہ کی جو تعریفیں معلوم ہوتی ہیں وہ ان کی بعض اقسام کو شامل ہیں لیکن بعض کو شامل نہیں مثلا نکرہ کی جو تعریف وجہ حصر سے معلوم ہوئی ہے یہ اس نمرہ کو شامل ہے جو فرد پر دال ہے لیکن جو مفہوم پر دال ہے اس کو شامل نہیں لہذا ضرورت پیش آئیکہ اس وجہ حصر کے علاوہ ایسی تعریف کی جائے کہ جو معرفہ اور نکرہ کی جمیع اقسام کو شامل ہونکرہ کی تعریف کی ماوضع لشی لا بعینہ الخ اور معرفہ کی تعریف کی ماوضع لشی بعینہ الخ ان تعریفوں میں شئی کا لفظ مذکور ہے اور شی عام ہے مفہوم ہو یا فرد ہو۔
His statement 'fahima ma wudi'a': By this expression the commentator answers an implied question. Question: From the second division, the derived types are eight. Then what interest does the author have in ma'rifah and nakirah, such that he defined only these two and did not define the remaining types? Answer: The reason is that the definitions of ma'rifah and nakirah that are known from the basis of partition (wajh al-hasr) cover some of their subtypes but not others. For example, the definition of nakirah that is known from the basis of partition applies to the nakirah that indicates an individual, but not to the one that indicates a meaning. Hence the need arises to define them with a definition that includes all types of ma'rifah and nakirah, beyond that basis. So the definition of nakirah is 'what is placed for a thing not specified' (ma wudi'a li shay'in la bi-'aynihi) and the definition of ma'rifah is 'what is placed for a thing specified' (ma wudi'a li shay'in bi-'aynihi). In these definitions, the word 'thing' (shay') is mentioned, and shay' is general, whether meaning or individual.
Source pages p. 532

Commentary
This passage explains why the author of al-Tawdih (Sadr al-Shari'ah) defines ma'rifah and nakirah using the word 'shay'' (thing) rather than restricting them to individuals. The eight types from the second division (presumably a prior classification) include both nouns that indicate individuals (like proper nouns) and nouns that indicate meanings (like abstract nouns). The definitions derived from the basis of partition (wajh al-hasr) only cover the individual-indicating types. To include all subtypes, the author defines nakirah as 'what is placed for a thing not specified' and ma'rifah as 'what is placed for a thing specified'. The word 'thing' (shay') is comprehensive, covering both meaning (mafhum) and individual (fard). This ensures the definitions are exhaustive. This is a standard Hanafi usul definition that emphasizes the semantic range of these categories.

Key Terms
معرفة (ma'rifah): A noun coined to be used for a specified thing (shay' bi-'aynihi).
نكرة (nakirah): A noun coined to be used for a thing not specified (shay' la bi-'aynihi).

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) To include both meanings and individuals
C) To exclude abstract nouns from the definitions
D) To follow a Shafi'i convention

Key Takeaway
Hanafi usul definitions of ma'rifah and nakirah are expanded using 'shay'' to encompass all subtypes, including those indicating meanings.

Is Definiteness Based on Coinage or the Listener?
Critique of a proposed condition in the definition of ma'rifah and nakirah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi position that definiteness (ta'yin) is determined by coinage (wad') rather than the listener's knowledge by analyzing the objection to the condition 'ind al-itlaq li al-sami'.

Core Idea
Definiteness is a function of a word's coinage, not the listener's awareness—'jā'anī rajul' remains nakirah even if the hearer knows the man.

The definition of ma'rifah and nakirah seems straightforward, but a proposed condition—'upon absolute usage for the listener'—sparks a critical refinement. This lesson unpacks the debate: does definiteness depend on the word's inherent coinage or on the listener's context? The Hanafi position holds that coinage alone determines category, and we will see why.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposed condition
The initial claim adds 'ind al-itlaq li al-sami' to the definition of ma'rifah and nakirah. Understand what this condition means and why it was proposed (to account for specification from the listener's perspective).
The condition is that definiteness is determined at the time of utterance, from the listener's viewpoint.
2
Understand the commentator's objection
The commentator argues that the condition is unnecessary because coinage (wad') already determines specification. If a word is coined to indicate a specific thing, it is ma'rifah; otherwise nakirah, regardless of the listener's knowledge.
3
Analyze the example
Consider the sentence 'jā'anī rajul' (a man came to me). Even if the listener knows exactly which man, 'rajul' remains nakirah because its coinage does not indicate specification. This demonstrates that coinage, not listener knowledge, governs definiteness.
4
Evaluate the rationale
The Hanafi position relies on the principle that linguistic categories are defined by the word's coinage, not by contextual factors. This approach ensures consistency and avoids subjective variation based on the hearer's awareness.
This is a key principle in Hanafi usul: the indication of the word (dalalat al-lafz) is primary.

دونوں جمع ہو سکتے ہیں مثال کے طور پر لفظ مین ہے اگر یہ لحاظ کر لیں کہ یہ وضع ہے چشمے کے لیے سونے کے لیے قوت باصرہ کے لیے تو یہ مشترک ہے اور اگر یہ لحاظ کر لیا جائے کہ یہ موضوع ہے قوت باصرہ کے جمیع افراد کے لیے تو یہ عام ہے اور اگر حیثیتیں میں منافات ہو تو اسکی مثال جیسے وضع للکثیر غیر محصور اور وضع للواحد یا وضع للكثير محصور کے درمیان منافات ہے یہی وجہ ہے کہ یوں نہیں ہو سکتا ہے کہ ایک لفظ ایک حیثیت سے عام ہو اور ایک حیثیت سے خاص ہو۔ و ماذکر :۔ یہ شرح کی عبارت کا دوسرا حصہ ہے غرض سوال مقدر کا جواب ہے۔ سوال:۔ صاحب توضیح اور دوسرے علماء تو یہ کہتے ہیں کہ نکرہ موصوفہ من وجہ عام ہے اور من وجہ خاص ہے اب نکرہ موصوفہ ایک ہی لفظ ہے جو خاص بھی ہے اور عام بھی ہے جبکہ تم نے کہا ہے کہ یہ نہیں ہوسکتا کہ ایک ہی لفظ ایک حیثیت سے عام ہو اور ایک حیثیت سے خاص ہو۔
The condition of 'ind al-itlaq li al-sami' and the commentator's objection upon it. His saying 'ind al-itlaq li al-sami': First, it is explained that the condition 'ind al-itlaq li al-sami' is attached to both ma'rifah and nakirah: to ma'rifah with specification and to nakirah with non-specification. Why is this condition imposed? Because the existence of specification in ma'rifah and non-specification in nakirah is not based on the coinage (wad') but on the time of utterance, and specification or non-specification is not from the perspective of the speaker but from the perspective of the listener. Upon this the commentator objects. Objection: There is no need to impose the condition 'ind al-itlaq li al-sami', because ma'rifah is said of a noun that has been coined to be used for a specified thing, and 'thing' is general, whether meaning or individual. And nakirah is said of a noun that has been coined to be used for a non-specified thing. So specification and non-specification are considered according to coinage, due to the indication of the word. If the word indicates specification, it is ma'rifah; if it indicates non-specification, it is nakirah. So there is no need to say 'according to the listener', because it is possible that when a person says "jā'anī rajul" (a man came to me), in that, even if the man is specified in the listener's mind, it remains nakirah because the word does not indicate specification. The necessity of qualifications of aspects (haythiyyat). His saying 'wa a'lam': This is the first part of the commentary's expression. The purpose is the text of al-Tawdih, that the commentator says that the author of al-Tawdih said that it is necessary to consider the qualification of aspect in these types so that the illusion of intrinsic contradiction does not occur. And aspects are of two types: sometimes there is conflict between them and sometimes not. If there is no conflict, its example is 'amm and mushtarak: 'amm is coined for many meanings (or: many individuals) and mushtarak is coined for a single meaning with many individuals (or: many meanings). Hence there is no conflict between them, and therefore they can be combined; for example, the word 'ayn: if we consider that it is coined for spring, for gold, for the faculty of sight, then it is mushtarak; and if we consider that it is coined for all individuals of the faculty of sight, then it is 'amm. And if aspects conflict, its example is like the conflict between being coined for many non-limited individuals and being coined for a single individual or for many limited individuals. This is why it is not possible that a single word be 'amm from one aspect and khas from another. And his saying 'wa madhkur': This is the second part of the commentary's expression; it is an answer to an implied question. Question: The author of al-Tawdih and other scholars say that nakirah mawsufah (qualified indefinite) is in one respect 'amm and in one respect khas. Now nakirah mawsufah is a single word that is both khas and 'amm, whereas you have said that it is not possible for a single word to be 'amm from one aspect and khas from another.
Source pages p. 533

Commentary
This passage contains two related discussions. First, the condition 'ind al-itlaq li al-sami' (upon absolute usage for the hearer) in the definition of ma'rifah and nakirah is critiqued. The initial explanation claims that definiteness depends on the time of utterance and the listener's perspective, not on coinage. The commentator objects: definiteness is based on the coinage (wad') of the word; if the word indicates specification, it is ma'rifah, otherwise nakirah, regardless of the listener's knowledge. Example: 'jā'anī rajul' is nakirah even if the hearer knows which man. Second, the need for aspectual qualifications (haythiyyat) is explained. Types like 'amm and mushtarak can coexist in one word if considered from different aspects (e.g., 'ayn can be mushtarak for multiple meanings and 'amm for all individuals of one meaning). If aspects conflict (e.g., 'amm for unlimited and khas for limited), combination is impossible. This addresses the question of how nakirah mawsufah can be both 'amm and khas: from different aspects, it is possible. This is a key Hanafi usul principle about the compatibility of categories when considered from different perspectives.

Key Terms
عند الإطلاق للسامع ('ind al-itlaq li al-sami): A proposed condition meaning 'upon absolute usage for the listener' in the definition of ma'rifah and nakirah.
وضع (wad'): Coinage or linguistic assignation; the original fixing of a word to a meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator's objection, why is the condition 'ind al-itlaq li al-sami' unnecessary?
A) Because definiteness depends only on the speaker's intention
B) Because the coinage of the word determines specification, not the listener's knowledge
C) Because all nouns are indefinite when first mentioned
D) Because the listener always knows the referent

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position is that definiteness (ma'rifah/nakirah) is determined by the word's coinage, not by the listener's or speaker's context.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

Reconciling Categories through Aspectual Qualifications (Haythiyyat)
Explanation of aspectual qualifications to avoid intrinsic contradiction in types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Hanafi concept of haythiyyah (aspectual qualification) resolves apparent contradictions between linguistic categories such as 'amm and mushtarak, and apply it to the case of nakirah mawsufah.

Core Idea
A single word can be both 'amm and mushtarak when considered from different aspects—haythiyyah prevents false contradictions.

Linguistic categories like 'amm, khass, and mushtarak seem mutually exclusive, yet scholars sometimes describe a word as both 'amm and khas. How is this possible? The solution lies in aspectual qualifications (haythiyyat): a word may belong to different categories when viewed from different perspectives. This lesson unpacks the two types of aspectual relationships—compatible and conflicting—and applies them to the classic problem of nakirah mawsufah.

Guided Path
1
Understand the need for aspectual qualifications
The author of al-Tawdih states that considering aspects (haythiyyat) is necessary to avoid the illusion of intrinsic contradiction when a word appears to belong to multiple categories. Without aspects, categories would seem to conflict.
Haythiyyah means 'from a certain perspective'.
2
Identify the two types of aspectual relationships
Aspects can be either compatible (no conflict) or conflicting. If there is no conflict, categories can coexist in one word. If there is conflict, they cannot.
Compatibility: e.g., 'amm and mushtarak can coexist because they describe different features of the word.
3
Apply to 'amm and mushtarak using the example of 'ayn
The word 'ayn can be mushtarak when viewed as having multiple distinct meanings (eye, spring, gold). It can be 'amm when viewed as covering all individuals of one meaning (all eyes). From different haythiyyat, the same word can be both.
Note: The text seems to conflate 'amm (individuals of one meaning) with mushtarak (multiple meanings), but the example works.
4
Apply to the problem of nakirah mawsufah
An implied question: nakirah mawsufah (e.g., 'rajulun karimun') is said to be 'amm from one aspect and khas from another. The text answers that this is possible because the aspects are compatible (no conflict). The word is 'amm in terms of its indefinite nature covering all such individuals, and khas in terms of the qualifier restricting it.

Source Text

عند الاطلاق للسامع کی قید اور شاریخ کا اس پر اعتراض قوله عند الاطلاق للسامع : اولا یہ بتایا کہ عند الاطلاق للسامع کی قید معرفہ کے ساتھ بھی لگتی ہے اور نکرہ کے ساتھ بھی معرفہ میں تعیین کے ساتھ اور نکرہ میں عدم تعین کے ساتھ باتی یہ قید کیوں لگائی؟ اس لیے کہ معرفہ میں تعین کا ہونا اور نکرہ میں عدم تعین کا ہوتا اس کا مدار وضعپر نہیں بلکہ تلفظ کے وقت پر ہے اور تعیین ار عدم تعیین متکلم کے اعتبار سے نہیں بلکہ سامع کے لحاظ سے ہے اس پر شاریخ اعتراض کر رہے ہیں۔ اعتراض: عند الاطلاق للسامع کی قید لگانے کی ضرورت نہیں ہے اس لیے کہ معرفہ کہا جاتا ہے ایسے اسم کو جس کو وضع کیا گیا ہوتا کہ اس کو استعمال کیا جائے شی معین میں آگے شی میں تعمیم ہے مفہوم ہو یا فرد ہو۔ اور نکرہ ایسے اسم کو کہا جاتا ہے جس کو وضع کیا گیا ہوتا کہ استعمال کیا جائے بھی غیر معین میں پیس تعین او عدم تعین کا اعتبار بحسب الوضع ہے دلالت لفظ کی وجہ سے اگر لفظ دال ہو تعیین کے اعتبار سے تو معرفہ ہے اور اگر لفظ دال ہو غیر معین کے اعتبار سے تو یہ نکرہ ہے تو عند السامع کہنے کی ضرورت نہیں ہے کیونکہ ہو سکتا ہے کہ جب آدمی جاءنی رجل کہے تو اس میں شارخ کہتے ہیں کہ صاحب توضیح نے فرمایا تھا کہ ان اقسام میں حیثیت کی قید کا لحاظ کرنا ضروری ہے تا کہ تباین ذاتی کا وہم پیدا نہ ہو اور حیثیت کی دوقسمیں ہیں کہ کبھی ان کے درمیان منافات ہوگئی اور کبھی نہیں Amm and Khass — General and Specific
