The Specific (al-Khass) and Its Definitive Ruling
Definition of al-khass and its definitive implication, with application to the qur' dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a specific word (khass) and explain why it obligates the ruling definitively, and apply this principle to resolve the dispute over the meaning of 'qur' in the verse.

Core Idea
The specific (khass) in Hanafi usul obligates the ruling definitively; polysemy does not override unless a clear context forces it.

This lesson introduces the usul al-fiqh concept of al-khāṣṣ (the specific). A specific word, when considered purely as specific, imposes its ruling definitively (qat'an). This definitive nature is not absolute probability-free, but means no probability arises from a credible proof. We will examine how this principle is used to argue for the Hanafi interpretation of the word 'qur' in the Qur'an, demonstrating the practical application of usul rules.

Guided Path
1
Define al-khāṣṣ
Understand the definition: a word that is specific in meaning and, when considered without obstacles like metaphorical context, obligates the ruling it indicates definitively.
The text gives example: 'Zayd is a scholar' where 'Zayd' is specific.
2
Understand 'definitively' (qat'an)
Recognise the two meanings of 'definitive'. Here it means no probability arising from a proof (lā iḥtimāl nāshi' 'an dalīl), not absolute impossibility of probability.
This is a key Hanafi nuance.
3
Apply to the 'iddah verse
The word 'three' (thalāthata) is specific (khāṣṣ). According to the rule, it must obligate a definitive count. The dispute is whether 'qur' means menstruation or purity. The Hanafi argument: if 'qur' meant purity, the count would become ambiguous, violating the definitive nature of 'three'. Therefore, 'qur' must mean menstruation.
Otherwise, you could get two or three purities depending on counting the purity in which divorce occurred.
4
Identify the practical result
The thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafis rule the 'iddah as three menstrual cycles; Shafi'is rule it as three clean periods.
This shows how a usul principle directly impacts fiqh rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ : الْخَاصٌ مِنْ حَيْثُ هُوَ خَاصٌ ( أَىْ مِنْ غَيْرِ اعْتِبَارِ الْعَوَارِضِ وَالْمَوَانِعِ كَالْقَرِينَةِ الصَّارِفَةِ عَنْ إِرَادَةِ الْحَقِيقَةِ مَثَلًا .. ( يُوجِبُ الْحُكْمَ فَإِذَا قُلْنَا زَيْدٌ عَالِمٌ فَزَيْدٌ خَاصٌ فَيُوجِبُ الْحُكْمَ بِالْعِلْمِ عَلَى زَيْدٍ وَأَيْضًا الْعَالِمُ لَفَظٌ خَاصٌ بِمَعْنَاهُ فَيُوجِبُ الْحُكْمَ بِذَلِكَ الْأَمْرِ الْخَاصْ عَلَى زَيْدٍ . ( قَطْعًا ) وَسَيَجِيءُ أَنَّهُ يُرَادُ بِالْقَطْعِ مَعْنَيَانِ وَالْمُرَادُ هَهُنَا الْمَعْنَى الْأَعَمُ ، وَهُوَ أَنْ لَا يَكُونَ لَهُ احْتِمَالُ نَاشِئٌ عَنْ دَلِيلٍ لَا أَنْ لَا يَكُونَ لَهُ احْتِمَالٌ أَصْلًا . ( فَفِي قَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى ( وَالْمُطَلَّقَاتُ يَتَرَبَّصْنَ بِأَنْفُسِهِنَّ ثَلَاثَةَ قُرُوءٍ ) لَا يُحْمَلُ الْقُرْءُ عَلَى الطُّهْرِ ) وَإِلَّا فَإِنِ احْتُسِبَ الطُّهْرُ الَّذِي طَلَّقَ فِيهِ يَجِبُ طُهْرَانِ ، وَبَعْضٌ وَإِنْ لَمْ يُحْتَسَبْ تَجِبُ ثَلَاثَةٌ وَبَعْضٌ . اعْلَمْ أَنَّ الْقُرْءَ لَفْظٌ مُشْتَرَكٌ وُضِعَ لِلْحَيْضِ ، وَوُضِعَ لِلطَّهْرِ فَفِي قَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى ) وَالْمُطَلَّقَاتُ يَتَرَبَّصْنَ بِأَنْفُسِهِنَّ ثَلاثَةَ قُرُوءٍ ( الْمُرَادُ مِنَ الْقُرْءِ الْحَيْضُ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى وَالطُّهْرُ عِنْدَ الشَّافِعِي رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى فَنَحْنُ نَقُولُ لَوْ كَانَ الْمُرَادُ الطُّهْرَ لَبَطَلَ مُوجِبُ الْخَاصِ وَهُوَ لَفْظُ ثَلَاثَةِ ؛ لِأَنَّهُ لَوْ كَانَ الْمُرَادُ الطُّهْرَ ، وَالطَّلَاقُ الْمَشْرُوعُ هُوَ الَّذِي يَكُونُ فِي حَالَةِ الطُّهْرِ فَالطُّهْرُ الَّذِي طَلَّقَ فِيهِ إِنْ لَمْ يُحْتَسَبْ مِنْ الْعِدَّةِ يَجِبُ ثَلَاثَةُ أَطْهَارٍ وَبَعْضُ وَإِنْ اُحْتُسِبَ كَمَا هُوَ مَذْهَبُ
Section: The specific (al-khāṣṣ) inasmuch as it is specific (i.e., without considering obstacles and preventers, such as a context that turns away from the literal meaning). It obligates the ruling. So when we say 'Zayd is a scholar', 'Zayd' is specific, and it obligates the ruling of knowledge on Zayd. Also, 'al-'ālim' is a specific word in its meaning, so it obligates the ruling of that specific matter on Zayd. Definitively. It will come that by 'definitive' two meanings are intended, and what is meant here is the more general meaning: that there is no probability arising from a proof, not that there is no probability at all. So in the saying of the Exalted: 'And divorced women shall wait for three qurū,' 'qur' cannot be interpreted as purity (ṭuhr). Otherwise, if the purity in which divorce occurred is counted, two purities are required; and if it is not counted, three and a part (are required). Know that 'qur' is a polysemous word placed for menstruation and placed for purity. In His saying '…three qurū,' the intended meaning of 'qur' is menstruation according to Abū Ḥanīfah (may Allah have mercy on him) and purity according to al-Shāfiʿī (may Allah have mercy on him). We say: if purity were intended, then the implication of the specific (khāṣṣ), which is the word 'three', would be invalidated. Because if purity were intended, and the legislated divorce is that which occurs in the state of purity, then the purity in which divorce occurred, if not counted from the 'iddah, three purities and a part are required; and if counted, as is the school (…).
Source pages p. 534

Commentary
This is a short, dense chapter (faṣl) on the concept of al-khāṣṣ (the specific) in usul al-fiqh. The author defines al-khāṣṣ as a word that, when considered purely as specific (ignoring external factors like metaphorical context), obligates the ruling it indicates definitively (qaṭʿan). The definitive nature here does not mean absolute impossibility of probability, but rather that there is no probability arising from any credible proof. The text then provides a practical example from the Qur'an: the word 'thalaatha' (three) is a specific word indicating the number three definitively. The word 'qur'' is polysemous, meaning both menstruation and purity. The Hanafi school, following Abu Hanifah, holds that in the verse 'And divorced women shall wait for three qurū,' the meaning is three menstrual periods. The Shafi'i school interprets it as three clean periods. The Hanafi argument is that if 'qur'' meant purity (ṭuhr), then the definitive implication of the specific word 'three' would be lost. This is because the purity in which divorce occurs might or might not be counted toward the 'iddah, leading to ambiguity in the number. Preserving the definitive nature of the specific word 'three' requires interpreting 'qur'' as menstruation, which gives a clear count. This reasoning exemplifies how usul principles (definitive implication of the specific) are applied to resolve legal disputes.

Key Terms
خاص (khāṣṣ): A word that is specific in meaning and obligates the ruling definitively when considered purely as specific, without regard to preventers like metaphorical context.
قطعة (qat'an): Definitively, meaning that there is no probability arising from a proof (lā iḥtimāl nāshi' 'an dalīl), not absolute absence of probability.
قرء (qur'): A polysemous word meaning both menstruation (ḥayḍ) and purity (ṭuhr), but in the verse it is interpreted as menstruation by Abu Hanifah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi argument in the passage, why must 'qur' mean menstruation?
A) Because it is the literal meaning
B) To preserve the definitive nature of the specific word 'three'
C) Because the Shafi'i view is linguistically weak
D) Because of consensus among the Companions

Key Takeaway
The specific word (khass) in Hanafi usul obligates the ruling definitively (qat'an), and this principle is used to resolve interpretational disputes in the Quran.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
10 min
Khass and Certainty: The Quru' Dispute
Applying khass principle to quru' dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that khass yields certainty (qat') to resolve the quru' dispute and evaluate the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Khass yields certainty unless a diverting context exists; the quru' dispute shows that interpreting a homonym can undermine that certainty.

This passage from al-Tarwīḥ expands on the definition of khass (specific) as necessitating a ruling with certainty. The commentator applies this principle to the famous dispute over the meaning of 'quru'' in the Qur'anic verse on divorce (2:228). Abu Hanifa interprets it as menstruation, while al-Shafi'i interprets it as purity. The Hanafi argument here is that accepting the Shafi'i reading would destroy the certainty that the specific numeral 'three' requires, because the count of purities would vary. The passage also provides a unique rebuttal against the claim that a part of purity counts as purity.

Guided Path
1
1. Recognize the definition of khass and its certainty
Identify the statement in the passage that khass (e.g., the word 'three') necessitates its ruling with certainty (qat'). Note that 'certainty' here means no probability arising from evidence, not absolute absence of probability.
This is the key usul principle being applied.
2
2. Identify the homonym 'quru'' and the two interpretations
Locate where the passage explains that 'quru'' is a homonym for menstruation (hayd) and purity (tuhur). Abu Hanifa takes it as hayd; al-Shafi'i as tuhur.
The homonymy makes the verse potentially ambiguous.
3
3. Understand the Hanafi argument that interpreting it as purity undermines the specific numeral
Follow the reasoning: If 'quru'' means purity, then the waiting period would be either two purities plus a part (if the initial purity is counted) or three plus a part (if not counted). This variability contradicts the certainty that the specific word 'three' should provide.
The Hanafi claim is that the Shafi'i reading nullifies the implication (mu'jib) of the khass.
4
4. Analyze the rebuttal against the Shafi'i objection
The passage anticipates the objection that 'a part of purity is purity'. The Hanafi response is that if a part sufficed, the third period could be partial, allowing marriage before completing three full purities, which violates consensus.
This rebuttal is presented as decisive and unique.

Source Text

جواب:۔ یہ ضابطہ جو بیان کیا گیا ہے یہ اس عام اور خاص کے بارے میں ہے۔ جو حقیقی ہوں اور نکرہ موصوفہ کا عام خاص ہونا حقیقی نہیں ہے بلکہ مجازی ہے هذا غالبیة : ۔ یہ شرح کی عبارت کا آخری حصہ ہے غرض اس طرف اشارہ کرنا ہے کہ اس تقسیم سے حاصل ہونے والی اقسام میں آخری درجہ کی کوشش جو میں نے کر دی ہے لیکن مقام ابھی بھی اس قابل ہے کہ اس میں مزید غور و فکر کریں۔

فَصْلٌ : الْخَاصٌ مِنْ حَيْثُ هُوَ خَاصٌ ( أى مِنْ غَيْرِ اعْتِبَارِ الْعَوَارِضِ وَالْمَوَانِعِ كَالْقَرِينَةِ الصَّارِفَةِ عَنْ إِرَادَةِ الْحَقِيقَةِ مَثَلًا .. ( يُوجِبُ الْحُكْمَ فَإِذَا قُلْنَا زَيْدٌ عَالِمٌ فَزَيْدٌ خَاصٌ فَيُوجِبُ الْحُكْمَ بِالْعِلْمِ عَلَى زَيْدٍ وَأَيْضًا الْعَالِمُ لَفَظٌ خَاصٌ بِمَعْنَاهُ فَيُوجِبُ الْحُكْمَ بِذَلِكَ الْأَمْرِ الْخَاصٌ عَلَى زَيْدٍ . ( قطعًا ) وَسَيَجِيءُ أَنَّهُ يُرَادُ بِالْقَطْعِ مَعْنَيَانِ وَالْمُرَادُ هَهُنَا الْمَعْنَى الْاعَمُ ، وَهُوَ أَنْ لَا يَكُونَ لَهُ احْتِمَالُ نَاشِءٌ عَنْ دَلِيلٍ لَا أَنْ لَا يَكُونَ لَهُ احْتِمَالٌ أَصْلًا . ( فَفِي قَوْله تَعَالَى ( ثَلَاثَةَ قُرُوء ) لَا يُحْمَلُ الْقُرُءُ عَلَى الظُّهْرِ) وَإِلَّا فَإِنِ احْتَسِبَ الظُّهْرُ الَّذِى طَلَّقَ فِيهِ يَجِبُ طُهُرَانِ ، وَبَعْضٌ وَإِنْ لَمْ يُحْتَسَبُ تَجِبُ ثَلَاثَةٌ وَبَعْضٌ . اعْلَمُ أَنَّ الْقُرْء لَفَظْ مُشْتَرَكْ وُضِعَ لِلْحَيْضِ ، وَوُضِعَ لِلطَّهْرِ فَفِي قَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى ) وَالْمُطَلَّقَاتُ يَتَرَبَّصْنَ بِأَنْفُسِهِنَّ ثَلاثَةَ قُرُوء.( الْمُرَادُ مِنَ الْقُرُءِ الْحَيْضُ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى وَالطُّهَرُ عِندَ الشَّافِعِي رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى فَنَحْنُ نَقُولُ لَوْ كَانَ الْمُرَادُ الطُّهُرَ لَبَطَلَ مُوجِبُ الْخَاصٌ وَهُوَ لَفْظُ ثَلاثَةِ ؛ لِأَنَّهُ لَوْ كَانَ الْمُرَادُ الطُّهُرَ ، وَالطَّلاقُ الْمَشْرُوعُ هُوَ الَّذِي يَكُونُ فِي حَالَةِ الظُّهْرِ فَالطُّهَرُ الَّذِى طَلَّقَ فِيهِ إِنْ لَمْ يُحْتَسَبُ مِنْ الْعِدَّةِ يَجِبُ ثَلاثَةُ أطْهَارٍ وَبَعْضُ وَإِنْ اُحْتُسِبَ كَمَا هُوَ مَذْهَبُ الشَّافِعِي يَجِبُ طُهُرَانِ وَبَعْضٌ .
( عَلَى أَنَّ بَعْضَ الظُّهْرِ لَيْسَ بِطُهْرٍ وَإِلَّا لَكَانَ الثَّالِثُ كَذَلِكَ) جَوَابٌ عَنْ سُؤَالٍ مُقَدْرٍ وَهُوَ أَنْ يُقَالَ لِمَ قُلْتُمُ إِنَّهُ إِذَا احْتُسِبَ يَكُونُ الْوَاجِبُ طُهُرَيْنِ وَبَعْضًا ، بَلُ الْوَاجِبُ ثَلاثَةٌ ؛ لأنَّ بَعْضَ الطَّهرِ طُهُرْ فَإِنَّ الظُّهَرَ أَدْنَى مَا يُطْلَقُ عَلَيْهِ لَفُظْ الظُّهْرِ وَهُوَ طُهُرُ سَاعَةٍ مَثَلًا فَتَقُولُ فِي جَوَابِهِ إِنَّ بَعْضَ الظُّهْرِ لَيْسَ بِطُهْرٍ لأَنَّهُ لَوْ كَانَ كَذَلِكَ لَا يَكُونُ بَيْنَ الْأَوَّلِ ، وَالثَّالِثِ فَرْق فَيَكْفِي فِى الثَّالِثِ بَعْضُ طُهْرٍ فَيَنبَغِى أَنَّهُ إذَا مَضَى مِنَ الثَّالِثِ شَيْءٍ يَحِلُّ لَهَا التَّزَوُّجُ ، وَهَذَا خِلافُ الإِسْمَاعِ ، وَهَذَا الْجَوَابُ قَاطِعٌ لِشُبُهَةِ الشَّافِعِيِّ رَحِمَهُ اللهُ ، وَقَدْ تَفَرَّدْتُ بِهَذَا .

ترجمہ: ( فصل خاص اس حیثیت سے کہ وہ خاص ہے ) یعنی بغیر اعتبار کرنے کے عوارض اور موانع کے مثل ایسے قرینہ کے جو پھیرنے والا ہے حقیقت کے ارادہ سے مثال کے طور پر ( ثابت کرتا ہے حکم کو ) پس جب کہا جائے زید عالم تو زید خاص ہے اپنے معنی کے ساتھ پس ثابت کرے گا علم کے حکم کا زید پر اور اسی طرح لفظ عالم خاص ہے پس ثابت کرے گا اس امر خاص کا حکم زید پر ( قطعی طور پر اور عنقریب آئے گا کہ مراد لیے جاتے ہیں قطعی کے دو معنی اور مراد یہاں معنی اہم ہے اور وہ یہ ہے کہ نہ ہو اس کے لیے ایسا احتمال جو پیدا ہونے والا دلیل سے نہ یہ کہ نہ ہو اس کے لیے کوئی احتمال بالکل (پس اللہ تعالیٰ کے قول ثلاثة قرو" میں محمول کیا جائے گا قرہ کو طہر پر وگر نہ پس اگر شمار کیا جائے اس طہر کو جس میں طلاق دی ہے تو واجب ہوں گے دو طہر اور بعض طہر اور اگر نہ شمار کیا جائے تو واجب ہوں گے تین اور بعض ) تو جان لے کہ بے شک قر والفظ مشترک ہے وضع کیا گیا ہے حیض کے لیے اور وضع کیا گیا ہے طہر کے لیے پس اللہ تعالیٰ کے قول والمطلقات متر بصن بانفسہن ثلثة قروء میں مراد قر و سے حیض ہے امام صاحب کے ہاں اور طہر ہے امام شافعی کے ہاں پس ہم کہتے ہیں کہ اگر مراد قرء سے طہر ہو تو البتہ باطل ہو جائے گا خاص کا موجب اور وہ لفظ ثلاثہ ہے کیونکہ اگر مراد طہر ہو اور وہ طلاق جو مشروع ہے وہ وہ ہے جو کہ ہو حالت طہر میں ہو پس وہ طہر جس میں طلاق دی گئی ہے اگر نہ شمار کیا جائے تو جیسا کہ وہ امام شافعی کا مذہب ہے تو واجب ہوں گے دو طہر اور بعض طہر (علاوہ ازین بے شک طہر کا بعض نہیں ظہر وگر نہ تو ہوگا تیسر اطہر بھی اسی طرح ) یہ جواب ہے سوال مقدر کا اور وہ یہ ہے کہ کہا جاتا ہے کہ کیوں کہا تم نے کہ بے شک جب شمار کیا جائے تو ہوں گے واجب دو طہر اور بعض بلکہ واجب ہوں گے تین
Answer: This rule that has been stated is about general and specific that are real, and the specificness of a described indefinite is not real but metaphorical. This is dominant. This is the last part of the commentary's expression. The aim is to indicate that among the divisions resulting from this division, the final level of effort that I have made, but the place is still worthy of more reflection.

Section: The specific insofar as it is specific (i.e., without considering accidents and preventers like a context that diverts from intending the literal meaning, for example) necessitates the ruling. So when we say 'Zaid is knowledgeable', 'Zaid' is specific, so it necessitates the ruling of knowledge upon Zaid. Also, 'the knowledgeable' is a specific word in its meaning, so it necessitates the ruling of that specific matter upon Zaid. (With certainty) It will come that by 'certainty' two meanings are intended, and here the broader meaning is intended, which is that it has no probability arising from evidence, not that it has no probability at all. So in Allah's statement 'three quru'', the word 'quru' is not carried upon purity; otherwise, if the purity in which divorce occurred is counted, two purities and a part are obligatory; and if not counted, three and a part are obligatory. Know that the word 'quru' is a homonym used for menstruation and used for purity. So in Allah's statement 'And divorced women shall wait for themselves for three quru'', the intended meaning of 'quru' is menstruation according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, and purity according to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him. So we say: If the intended meaning were purity, then the implication of the specific would be nullified, and that is the word 'three'; because if the intended meaning were purity, and the prescribed divorce is that which occurs in a state of purity, then the purity in which divorce occurred, if not counted from the waiting period, three full purities and a part are obligatory; and if counted, as is the school of al-Shafi'i, two purities and a part are obligatory.

(And that a part of purity is not purity; otherwise the third would be likewise) This is an answer to an anticipated question, which is: Why did you say that when it is counted, two purities and a part are obligatory? Rather, three are obligatory because part of purity is purity, since the minimum to which the word 'purity' applies is, for example, a purity of an hour. So you say in answer: A part of purity is not purity, because if it were so, there would be no difference between the first and the third, and a part of purity would suffice for the third, so it would be necessary that when a part of the third passes, it becomes permissible for her to marry, and this contradicts consensus. This answercuts off the doubt of al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, and I am unique in this.

Translation: (Section: The specific insofar as it is specific) i.e., without considering accidents and preventers like a context that diverts from intending the literal meaning, for example (necessitates the ruling) So when it is said 'Zaid is knowledgeable', Zaid is specific in its meaning, so it will establish the ruling of knowledge upon Zaid. Similarly, the word 'knowledgeable' is specific, so it will establish the ruling of that specific matter upon Zaid. (With certainty) And soon it will come that two meanings are intended by certainty, and here the broader meaning is intended, which is that it has no probability arising from evidence, not that it has no probability at all. (So in Allah's statement 'three quru'', the word 'quru' is interpreted as purity; otherwise, if the purity in which divorce occurred is counted, two purities and a part are obligatory; and if not counted, three and a part are obligatory.) Know that the word 'quru' is a homonym placed for menstruation and placed for purity. So in Allah's statement 'And divorced women shall wait for themselves for three quru', the intended meaning of 'quru' is menstruation according to Imam Abu Hanifa, and purity according to Imam al-Shafi'i. So we say: If the intended meaning from 'quru' were purity, then the implication of the specific, i.e., the word 'three', would be nullified; because if the intended meaning were purity, and the prescribed divorce is that which occurs in a state of purity, then the purity in which divorce was given, if not counted, as is the school of Imam al-Shafi'i, two purities and a part are obligatory. (Furthermore, a part of purity is not purity; otherwise the third purity would be likewise.) This is the answer to an anticipated question, which is: Why did you say that when it is counted, two purities and a part are obligatory? Rather, three are obligatory. Because part of purity is purity, since the minimum to which the word 'purity' applies is a purity of an hour, for example. So you say in answer: A part of purity is not purity, because if it were so, there would be no difference between the first and the third, and a part of purity would suffice for the third, so it should be that when a part of the third passes, marriage becomes permissible for her, and this contradicts consensus. This answer cuts off the doubt of al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, and I am unique in this.
Source pages p. 534

Commentary
This passage is an usul dispute focusing on the principle of 'khass' (specific) and its application to the interpretation of the word 'quru'' in the Qur'anic verse on divorce ('iddah). The passage is from the sharh (commentary) of al-Tarwīḥ, which expands on a section from the matn (al-Tawḍīḥ) concerning the definition of 'khass' as a term that yields certainty (qat'). The commentator begins with a brief introductory Urdu note discussing the previous division of 'amm' and 'khass'. Then the Arabic matn states that 'khass' insofar as it is specific, without considering extrinsic factors like context, necessitates a ruling with certainty. The commentator explains that 'certainty' here means no probability arising from evidence—not absolute lack of probability. The example given is the Qur'anic phrase 'thalāthata qurū'' (three periods). The word 'quru'' is a homonym meaning both menstruation and purity. Abu Hanifa interprets it as menstruation, al-Shafi'i as purity. The Hanafi argument is that if it meant purity, the specific number 'three' would lose its definitive implication because the waiting period would vary depending on whether the purity in which divorce occurred is counted. If counted, only two full purities plus a part would be required; if not, three plus a part. This inconsistency undermines the certainty that 'khass' requires. The commentator then addresses a potential objection: that a part of purity is itself purity. The answer is that a part of purity is not considered a full purity; otherwise, a partial third purity would suffice for the 'iddah, which contradicts the understood meaning of three complete purities. The commentator claims this answer is unique and decisive against al-Shafi'i's position. The Urdu translation repeats the same content. The passage thus demonstrates how a usul principle (khass implies certainty) is used to resolve a fiqh dispute between madhhabs.

Key Terms
خاص (khāṣṣ): A word that is specific in its reference, such as a proper noun or a word with a specific meaning; it necessitates its ruling with certainty (qat') barring any context that diverts from its literal meaning.
قرء (qur' (pl. qurū')): A homonym meaning both menstruation (hayd) and purity (tuhur). In the verse, Abu Hanifa takes it as menstruation, al-Shafi'i as purity.
قطع (qaṭ'): Certainty; here it means the absence of a probability arising from evidence, not absolute absence of any probability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi argument in this passage, why would interpreting 'quru'' as 'purity' nullify the implication of the specific word 'three'?
A) Because the word 'three' would then have to mean three menstruations, not three purities.
B) Because the waiting period would vary depending on whether the purity in which divorce occurred is counted or not, resulting in either two or three purities, which undermines the certainty required of a specific numeral.
C) Because Abu Hanifa said so, and his opinion must be followed in usul.
D) Because the word 'quru'' is only used for menstruation in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Khass yields certainty; if a homonym interpretation makes a specific numeral variable, the homonym must be resolved to preserve that certainty.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate

The Certainty of Khass: The Quru' Dispute
Analyzing khass and its certainty via the quru' example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the linguistic and textual reasoning behind the Hanafi interpretation of 'quru'' in Surah Al-Baqarah 2:228, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the principles of khass and ihtimal.

Core Idea
Khass is qat'i only when free from mu'arid and nasikh; the 'quru'' dispute shows how textual ambiguity can be resolved through internal consistency and ijma'.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Tawdih wa Al-Talwih takes us deep into the Hanafi usul principle that a specific text (khass) yields certainty of meaning and ruling, provided it is not overridden by a counter-proof or abrogation. The author illustrates this with the famous ikhtilaf over the interpretation of 'quru'' in the iddah verse. By working through the argument, you will see how Hanafi usul uses linguistic precision and logical consistency to defend its positions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source text and the homonym
Locate the Quranic verse (2:228) and note that the word 'quru'' is mushtarak (homonymous) with two possible meanings: hayd (menstruation) and tuhur (purity). This ambiguity triggers the need for takhsis.
2
State the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions
The Hanafi school holds that 'quru'' means hayd, while the Shafi'i school holds it means tuhur. This difference leads to different rulings for the waiting period (iddah).
3
Follow the Hanafi critique of the Shafi'i position
If 'quru'' means tuhur, then the specific number 'three' becomes problematic. The Shafi'i does not count the tuhur in which divorce occurred as one of the three, so only two complete tuhurs and a fraction of the third remain, contradicting the explicit 'three'. The Hanafi argues this inconsistency invalidates the Shafi'i interpretation.
4
Analyze the rebuttal against the rejoinder 'part of tuhur counts as tuhur'
A hypothetical objection: if part of tuhur counts as tuhur, then the third could be partial and marriage allowed prematurely. The Hanafi refutes this by noting it would violate ijma' (consensus) that the waiting period must be three complete cycles. Thus the objection fails.
5
Extract the usul principle
The entire argument demonstrates that khass (here the number 'three') necessitates a ruling that must be harmonized with the meaning of the homonym. The Hanafi resolution preserves the specificity of 'three' by interpreting 'quru'' as hayd, where each menstrual cycle is a complete period.

کہ احتمال ناشئ عن دلیل کی بھی نفی ہو۔
And that some of tuhur is not tuhur, otherwise the third would be like that. This is an answer to an implied question: why do you say that when counted, the obligatory is two tuhurs and part of a tuhur, but it is three? Because part of tuhur is tuhur, since the minimum to which the word tuhur applies is a tuhur of one hour, for example. So we say in answer: some of tuhur is not tuhur, because if that were the case there would be no difference between the first and the third, so in the third a part of tuhur would suffice, and it would be permissible for her to marry after some time has passed from the third, which is contrary to consensus. This answer cuts off the Shafi'i's doubt, and I am unique in this. Translation: (Fasl: khass from the aspect that it is specific) i.e., without considering incidental circumstances and preventers, such as a context that turns away from the literal meaning, it (establishes the ruling). So when it is said 'Zaid is knowledgeable', Zaid is specific with its meaning, so it establishes the ruling of knowledge on Zaid. Similarly the word 'knowledgeable' is specific, so it establishes the ruling of that specific matter on Zaid. (Definitively, and soon it will come that definitive has two meanings, and the intended meaning here is the more important one: that there is no probability arising from a proof, not that there is no probability at all.) (Then in Allah's saying 'three qurun' will be interpreted as tuhur, otherwise if that tuhur in which divorce occurred is counted, then two tuhurs and part of a tuhur are obligatory, and if not counted, three and part.) Know that the word quru' is homonymous, placed for hayd and placed for tuhur. So in Allah's saying 'and divorced women shall wait by themselves for three qurun', the intended meaning of quru' is hayd according to Imam (Abu Hanifa) and tuhur according to Imam al-Shafi'i. Then we say: if quru' means tuhur, then the implication of the specific is invalidated, and that is the word three. Because if tuhur is intended, and the divorce that is prescribed occurs in a state of tuhur, then if that tuhur is not counted, as is the Shafi'i's madhhab, then two tuhurs and part of a tuhur are obligatory. (Besides, some of tuhur is not tuhur, otherwise the third tuhur would be similar.) This is the answer to the implied question: why did you say that when counted, two tuhurs and part are obligatory, but rather three? Because part of tuhur is tuhur, since the minimum to which the word tuhur applies is a tuhur of one hour, for example. So we say in answer: some of tuhur is not tuhur, because if that were the case there would be no difference between the first and third, so in the third a part of tuhur would suffice, and it would be permissible for her to marry after some time has passed from the third, which is contrary to consensus. This answer terminates the Shafi'i's doubt, and I have indeed singularly [given this answer]. The ruling of khass. Explanation: The author of Tanqih is here stating the ruling of khass: that khass, by virtue of being specific, definitively necessitates [knowledge]. The author of Tawdih clarifies: 'From the aspect that it is specific' means: free from incidental circumstances and preventers. Free from incidental circumstances means that there is no opposing evidence; if opposing evidence appears, it will not necessitate the ruling. Preventers means no abrogator; if an abrogator is found, it will not necessitate the ruling. This is what is meant by 'al-qariyah al-sarifah'. 'Yujib al-hukm': 'hukm' here is general, whether linguistic or technical. Linguistic hukm refers to ascription, financial or otherwise; technical hukm is 'what is addressed'. The author gave a linguistic example: in 'Zaid is knowledgeable', 'Zaid' is specific, indicating a definite person, meaning the ruling is specific to him. 'Knowledgeable' is also specific, indicating a definite meaning of knowledge. Thus both establish that knowledge (a specific matter) is attributed to Zaid (a specific matter). 'Qat'an wa basiran': The word 'qat'an' means that there is no other probability in this specific. There are two types of probability: probability arising from a proof (dalil), and absolute probability (whether from a proof or not). Probability arising from a proof is specific, occurring only from a proof; absolute probability is general, could be from a proof or other. Here, probability arising from a proof is negated. Since the rule is that the negation of a specific is general, and the negation of a general is specific, therefore the negation of probability arising from a proof is general, and the negation of absolute probability is specific, because the negation of probability arising from a proof can occur without the negation of absolute probability. However, the negation of absolute probability necessarily includes the negation of probability arising from a proof.
Source pages p. 535

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis from a commentary (sharh) on Al-Tawdih wa Al-Talwih, focusing on the rule that a specific (khass) text, free from contextual counter-indications and abrogation, establishes a ruling with certainty. The author uses the famous ikhtilaf on the meaning of 'quru'' (قرء) in the Qur'anic verse of 'iddah (Surah al-Baqarah: 228) as the primary example. The Hanafi position is that 'quru'' means menstruation (hayd), while the Shafi'i holds it means purity (tuhur). The text argues that if 'quru'' meant purity, the number 'three' would become problematic: if the purity during which divorce occurred is not counted, the waiting period would not be three complete purities but two and a fragment, contradicting the explicit number. The author refutes the claim that a part of purity counts as a full purity, because if that were true, the first and third purities would be indistinguishable, and the woman could marry after just a fraction of the third purity, which contradicts ijma'. Hence, the Hanafi interpretation is upheld. The passage then provides a general explanation of khass, distinguishing between different types of probability (ihtimal) and emphasizing that the certainty of khass is only negated by a clear textual counter-proof (nasikh or mu'arid). This illustrates the Hanafi usul principle that 'amm is qat'i until specified, but khass is inherently qat'i.

Key Terms
خاص (khass): A word that indicates a specific individual or meaning (e.g., 'Zaid' or 'knowledge') and necessitates ruling definitively when free from opposing evidence and abrogation.
عام (amm): A word that covers multiple individuals without limit; its negation is specific. In this passage, the negation of a specific probability results in a general negation.
قروء (quru'): Plural of qur', a homonym meaning either menstruation (hayd) or purity (tuhur). The Hanafi position holds it means hayd in the iddah verse.
احتمال ناشئ عن دلیل (ihtimal nashi' 'an dalil): A probability that arises from a textual proof (e.g., a possible but not definitive interpretation). Negating this leaves room for other probabilities.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main argument the Hanafi author uses to refute the Shafi'i interpretation of 'quru''?
A) The Shafi'i interpretation is not based on any hadith.
B) If 'quru'' meant tuhur, the specific number 'three' would be contradicted because the waiting period would be two complete tuhurs and a fragment, not three.
C) The word 'quru'' cannot grammatically mean tuhur.
D) The Shafi'i interpretation was invented after the Prophet's time.

Key Takeaway
A specific text (khass) yields a certain ruling only if no counter-proof or abrogation exists; the 'quru'' dispute illustrates how Hanafi usul uses this principle to resolve interpretive conflicts.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

The Ruling of Khas: Language, Certainty, and Application to the Qur' Dispute
Explaining the ruling of khas and its application to the qur' dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that khas (specific) text yields certainty in meaning and derive the Hanafi position on the meaning of qur' based on the decisive number 'three'.

Core Idea
A khas term is decisive; the number 'three' demands exactly three periods — only the hayḍ interpretation satisfies.

In this passage, the commentator explains a core principle of Hanafi usul: a specific (khas) legal text, when free from opposing evidence and abrogation, imposes its ruling with certainty (qat'). This is not just a theoretical rule — it directly resolves a famous disagreement between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools over the meaning of 'qurū'' in Quran 2:228. By the end of this lesson, you will see how the precision of the word 'three' forces the interpretation to menstruation, because only that yields exactly three periods without addition or subtraction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the khas expression
Locate the word 'thalāthata' in the verse. Recognize it as khas because it indicates a specific number.
Khas can be a number, a named individual, or any term that limits meaning to a specific set.
2
State the Hanafi condition for khas to be decisive
The khas must be free from mu'āriḍ (opposing evidence) and nāsikh (abrogator). In this case, no such evidence exists.
If a mu'āriḍ appears, the khas may become subject to interpretation; if a nāsikh appears, it is replaced.
3
Apply the principle to the ambiguous word qur'
Since khas is decisive, the only permissible interpretation of qur' is the one that yields exactly three periods. Test both hayḍ and ṭuhr.
The commentator shows that ṭuhr leads to either deficiency or excess of the number three.
4
Conclude the Hanafi position
Hayḍ gives exactly three; ṭuhr does not. Therefore qur' means hayḍ.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf — the practical consequence of applying the usul rule.

ففى قوله تعالى ثلاثة قروء لا يحمل القروء: یہاں سے اتن خاص کے حکم پر پہلی تفریح بیان فرمار ہے ہیں جس سے پہلے یہ سمجھیں کہ لفظ " قر " مشترک ہے اس کے دو معنی آتے ہیں حیض اور طہر اب اللہ تعالیٰ کا ارشاد مبارک ہے والمطلقات تیتر بصن بالسن ثلث قرو: " اس میں امام ابو حنفیہ تقروء سے حیض مراد لیتے ہیں فرماتے ہیں کہ مطلقہ عورت کی عدت حیضوں کے ساتھ شمار کی جائے گی اور امام شافعی قروء سے طہر مراد لیتے ہیں۔ فرماتے ہیں کہ عورت کی عدت طہروں کے ساتھ شمار کی جائے گی۔
تفریح:۔ امام صاحب فرماتے ہیں کہ ثلاثہ قروء میں لفظ ثلاثہ خاص ہے۔ یہ ایک متعین عدد پر دلالت کرتا ہے جو دو سے او پر اور چار سے نیچے ہو۔ اور چونکہ خاص اپنے معنی میں حکم کو قطعا واجب کرتا ہے اس لیے یہاں قروء سے مراد وہی معنی لیں گے جس سے لفظ ثلاثہ کے موجب پر عمل ہو جائے۔ اور وہ اسی صورت میں ممکن ہے جب قروء سے مراد حیض لیا جائے اگر طہر مراد لیں تو ثلاثہ کے موجب پر عمل نہیں ہوسکتا اور وہ اس لیے کہ طلاق مسنون طہر میں ہوتی ہے۔ اب اگر کوئی آدمی اپنی بیوی کو طلاق طہر میں دیتا ہے تو یہ طہر جس میں طلاق واقع ہوئی ہے اس کو عدت کے طہروں میں شمار کیا جائے گا یا نہیں ؟ اگر اس کو شمار کیا جائے تو پھر ثلاثہ کے موجب پر عمل نہیں ہوتا بلکہ اس کے موجب سے کمی لازم آئے گی کیونکہ طلاق کے وقت ظہر کا کچھ نہ کچھ حصہ گزر چکا ہوگا اور بعض طہر باقی ہو گا تو جب اس کو عدت میں شمار کریں گے تو اس کے بعد والے دو طہر تو مکمل ہوں گے لیکن یہ طہر مکمل نہیں ہوگا تو عدت دو طہر اور تیسرے طہر کا کچھ حصہ ہوئی تو مثلثہ کے موجب پر عمل نہ ہوا اور اگر طلاق والے طبر کو عدت میں شمار نہ کیا جائے بلکہ اس کے بعد تین طہر عدت میں شمار کیے جائیں تو بھی ثلثہ کے موجب پر عمل نہیں ہو گا بلکہ عدت اس سے زیادہ لازم آئے گی کیونکہ تین طہر بعد والے ہو جائیں گے اور کچھ طہر یہ بھی ہو جائے گا جس میں طلاق ہوئی ہے تو یہ تین سے بڑھ گئے لہذا ثلثہ کے موجب پر عمل نہ ہوا اس لیے قروء سے طہر مراد لینا درست نہیں بلکہ قروء سے مراد حیض ہے اس صورت میں ثلثہ کے موجب پر عمل ہو جائے گا کیونکہ جس طہر میں طلاق ہوگی اس کے بعد والے متصل تین حیض عدت شمار کر لیے جائیں گے ۔ اس صورت میں ثلثہ سے نہ کمی ہوگی نہ زیادتی ۔
Because a part of tuhr is tuhr, because the minimum tuhr is the amount on which the word zahir is applied, and that tuhr is the duration of one moment, for example. So we say in response to it that indeed a part of tuhr is not tuhr, because if it were so, then there would be no difference between the first tuhr and the third tuhr; then a part of tuhr in the third tuhr would suffice. So it would be appropriate that when some of the third tuhr passes, it becomes permissible for her to marry, while this is contrary to consensus. And this answer nullifies the doubt of Imam al-Shafi'i, and indeed I am unique in this (response). 
The Ruling of Khas
Commentary: The author of Tanqih here states the ruling of khas: that khas, by virtue of being khas, necessarily necessitates knowledge definitively. The author of Tawdih explains: 'min haythu huwa khas' means that it is free from opposing factors (awāriḍ) and preventing factors (mawāni'). Free from opposing factors means that this khas has no opposing evidence; if an opposing evidence comes, it will not make the ruling necessary. Free from preventing factors means that there is no abrogator for it; if an abrogator is found, then also it will not make the ruling necessary. The phrase 'kā al-qaryata al-ṣārifiyya' refers to this same meaning. 'Yūjibu al-ḥukm': by 'ḥukm' is meant the general meaning, whether linguistically or terminologically. Linguistically, it means ascription of ownership etc.; terminologically, it means 'what is addressed'. And the author (Māīn) gave an example of linguistic ruling: in 'Zayd is knowledgeable', 'Zayd' is khas indicating a specific person, meaning the judgement is specific to him; and 'knowledgeable' is also khas indicating a specific meaning of knowledge. So from both it is established that here knowledge (which is a specific matter) is attributed to Zayd (which is a specific matter).
'Qat'an wa basījan...' 'Qat'an': this condition means that there is no other probability (iḥtimāl) in this khas. And probabilities are of two types: probability arising from evidence (iḥtimāl nāshi' 'an dalīl) and absolute probability (iḥtimāl muṭlaq), i.e., whether from evidence or without evidence. Among them, the probability arising from evidence is specific (khas) because it arises only from evidence, and the absolute probability is general (ʿāmm) because it can arise from evidence and from non-evidence. And here the denial is of the probability arising from evidence. And since it is a rule that the negation of a specific is general, and the negation of a general is specific, therefore the negation of probability arising from evidence will be general, and the negation of absolute probability will be specific, because the negation of probability arising from evidence can occur without the negation of absolute probability. But in the negation of absolute probability, it is necessary that the negation of probability arising from evidence also occurs.
'Wa al-murādu bi-dhālika al-ma'nā al-ʿāmm': From here it is stated that the probability that is denied in khas is the one whose negation is general, meaning that the denial is of probability arising from evidence, not of absolute probability. So the meaning is that in khas there is no such probability that arises from evidence, but absolute probability can exist, i.e., probability arising without evidence can be found.
First branch on the ruling of khas
'Fa fī qawlihi taʿālā thalāthatu qurū' lā yaḥmilu al-qurū'': From here, the first branch on the ruling of khas is explained. First understand that the word 'qur' is ambiguous; it has two meanings: hayḍ (menstruation) and ṭuhr (purity). Now Allah's blessed verse is 'Wa al-muṭallaqātu yatarabbaṣna bi-anfusihinna thalāthata qurū''. In this, Imam Abu Hanifa takes qurū' to mean hayḍ, saying that the waiting period of a divorced woman is counted with menstruations. And Imam al-Shafi'i takes qurū' to mean ṭuhr, saying that the waiting period of a woman is counted with tuhr periods.
Branch: Imam (Abu Hanifa) says that in 'thalāthata qurū', the word 'thalāthata' is khas. It indicates a specific number, which is above two and below four. And since khas necessarily necessitates its ruling definitively, therefore here we will take that meaning of qurū' by which the requirement of the word 'thalāthata' is fulfilled. And that is only possible when qurū' is taken to mean hayḍ. If ṭuhr is taken, then the requirement of 'thalāthata' cannot be fulfilled. That is because the recommended divorce occurs in a period of tuhr. Now if a man divorces his wife in a tuhr, will this tuhr in which divorce occurred be counted in the waiting period tuhrs? If it is counted, then the requirement of 'thalāthata' is not fulfilled, rather there will be less than its requirement, because at the time of divorce some part of the tuhr has passed and some part remains. When we count it in the waiting period, then the two tuhrs after it will be complete, but this tuhr will be incomplete. So the waiting period will be two tuhrs and part of a third tuhr, so the requirement of 'thalāthata' is not fulfilled. And if the tuhr of divorce is not counted, but three tuhrs after it are counted, then also the requirement of 'thalāthata' will not be fulfilled, rather the waiting period will be more than that, because three subsequent tuhrs plus the tuhr in which divorce occurred are four or more. Therefore, it is not correct to take qurū' to mean ṭuhr. Rather, the meaning of qurū' is hayḍ, because in this case the requirement of 'thalāthata' is fulfilled, since after the tuhr in which divorce occurs, the three consecutive menstruations will be counted as the waiting period. In this case, there is neither less nor more than the requirement of 'thalāthata'.
Source pages p. 536

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the ruling of khas (specific) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh, from the commentary on al-Tanqih and al-Tawdih. It explains that a khas text, by virtue of its specificity, imposes a ruling with certainty (qat') provided it is free from opposing evidence ('awāriḍ) and abrogation (mawāni'). The author clarifies the types of probability: one arising from evidence (iḥtimāl nāshi' 'an dalīl) and absolute probability (iḥtimāl muṭlaq). In khas, only the former is negated; absolute probability may still exist. The passage then applies this principle to the famous dispute over the meaning of 'qurū'' in Quran 2:228. Since the number 'three' (thalāthata) is khas and thus decisive, the only interpretation that satisfies the precise number three without addition or subtraction is that 'qurū'' means menstrual periods (hayḍ). This is the Hanafi position, as opposed to Imam al-Shafi'i's view that it means purity periods (tuhr). The commentary follows the order of the base text, first establishing the general rule, then demonstrating its application through this jurisprudential branch (tafrīʿ).

Key Terms
خاص (khas): A word or phrase that indicates a specific, limited meaning (e.g., a number, a named individual) and, when free from opposing evidence and abrogation, necessitates its ruling definitively.
احتمال (ihtimal): Probability or alternative interpretation. Two types: ihtimal nashi' 'an dalil (probability arising from evidence) and ihtimal mutlaq (absolute probability, including those without evidence).
قرء (qur'): A word with two possible meanings: menstruation (hayd) or purity period (tuhr). Used in Quran 2:228 for the waiting period of divorced women.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the Hanafi school interpret 'qurū'' as menstruations (hayḍ)?
A) Because the word qur' always means menstruation.
B) Because the number 'three' is khas and decisive; only hayḍ yields exactly three periods.
C) Because Imam Abu Hanifa said so without evidence.
D) Because the Shafi'i interpretation is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle that khas (specific) text yields certainty forces the interpretation of 'qurū'' to mean menstrual periods, because only that satisfies the decisive number 'three'.

The Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute over the Meaning of 'Qur''
Explaining the first branch of khass: the meaning of quru' and the Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi argument for interpreting 'qur'' as hayd based on the requirement of the specific number 'thalathah', and evaluate the Shafi'i counterargument based on grammatical gender agreement.

Core Idea
The specific number 'thalathah' forces the meaning of 'qur'' to hayd; only hayd yields exactly three complete units.

The word 'qur'' in the Quranic verse on 'iddah is homonymous, meaning both menstruation and purity. The Hanafi and Shafi'i schools disagree on which meaning is intended. This dispute is a classic case in usul al-fiqh about how to resolve ambiguity when a specific number (khass) is involved. In this lesson, we will dissect the Hanafi argument that the requirement of exactness obligates the meaning of hayd, and the Shafi'i reliance on grammatical rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the homonym and the two possible meanings
Recognize that 'qur'' is a homonymous word (mushtarak) with two meanings: hayd and tuhr. The Quran uses it in the context of 'iddah.
Homonyms require context or additional evidence to determine the intended meaning.
2
Analyze the Hanafi argument from the specific number
Imam Abu Hanifa argues that the word 'thalathah' (three) is a specific number (khass) that must be fulfilled exactly. If 'qur'' meant tuhr, the first tuhr is incomplete because divorce occurs during it, leading to a deficient or excessive count. Only if 'qur'' means hayd can three complete menstruations be counted after the divorce.
The Hanafi position prioritizes the literal fulfillment of the number over grammatical gender rules in this case.
3
Examine the Shafi'i proof from grammatical gender
Al-Shafi'i invokes the rule that a feminine number (thalathah) requires a masculine tamyiz. Since 'qur'' as tuhr is masculine in meaning, it fits the rule, whereas hayd is feminine in meaning, creating a grammatical mismatch.
The Shafi'i position prioritizes grammatical consistency.
4
Understand the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i proof
The Hanafi school responds that hayd, though feminine in meaning, is masculine in its word-form, so it can serve as tamyiz for a feminine number without violating the rule. Thus, the grammatical objection is neutralized.
The Hanafi response relies on the distinction between semantic gender and morphological gender.
5
Consider the objection and its Hanafi answer
An objection is raised: if divorce occurs during hayd, the same problem of incomplete count arises. The Hanafi answer distinguishes between the recommended divorce (talaq sunni, which occurs in tuhr) and the irregular divorce (talaq bid'i, in hayd). For the latter, a method of partial counting ensures exactly three menstruations.
This shows the Hanafi school addresses edge cases systematically.

والمراد بنا المعنى الأعم: يہاں سے يہ فرما رہے ہیں کہ خاص میں جس احتمال کی نفی کی گئی ہے وہ وہی ہے جس کی نفی عام ہے یعنی احتمال ناشی عن دلیل کی نفی کی گئی ہے احتمال مطلق کی نفی نہیں کی گئی تو مطلب يہ ہوا کہ خاص میں غیر کا ایسا احتمال تو نہیں ہوتا جو دلیل سے پیدا ہو لیکن احتمال مطلق ہو سکتا ہے یعنی جو احتمال بغیر دلیل کے پیدا ہو وہ پایا جا سکتا ہے۔
خاص کے حکم پر پہلی تفريع
ففي قوله تعالى: {والمطلقات يتربصن بأنفسهن ثلاثة قروء} لا يحمل القروء على الطهر: يہاں سے خاص کے حکم پر پہلی تفريع بيان فرما رہے ہیں جس سے پہلے يہ سمجھيں کہ لفظ "قرء" مشترک ہے اس کے دو معنی آتے ہيں: حيض اور طہر۔ اب اللہ تعالیٰ کا ارشاد مبارک ہے: {والمطلقات يتربصن بأنفسهن ثلاثة قروء}۔ اس میں امام ابو حنيفہ "قروء" سے حيض مراد ليتے ہيں، فرماتے ہيں کہ مطلقہ عورت کی عدت حيضوں کے ساتھ شمار کی جائے گی۔ اور امام شافعی "قروء" سے طہر مراد ليتے ہيں، فرماتے ہيں کہ عورت کی عدت طہروں کے ساتھ شمار کی جائے گی۔
تفريع: امام صاحب فرماتے ہيں کہ "ثلاثة قروء" میں لفظ "ثلاثة" خاص ہے۔ يہ ایک متعين عدد پر دلالت کرتا ہے جو دو سے اوپر اور چار سے نيچے ہو۔ اور چونکہ خاص اپنے معنی میں حکم کو قطعاً واجب کرتا ہے اس لیے يہاں "قروء" سے مراد وہی معنی ليں گے جس سے لفظ "ثلاثة" کے موجب پر عمل ہو جائے۔ اور وہ اسی صورت میں ممکن ہے جب "قروء" سے مراد حيض ليا جائے۔ اگر طہر مراد ليں تو "ثلاثة" کے موجب پر عمل نہيں ہو سکتا، اور وہ اس لیے کہ طلاق مسنون طہر میں ہوتی ہے۔ اب اگر کوئی آدمی اپنی بيوی کو طلاق طہر میں ديتا ہے تو يہ طہر جس میں طلاق واقع ہوئی ہے اس کو عدت کے طہروں میں شمار کيا جائے گا يا نہيں؟ اگر اس کو شمار کيا جائے تو پھر "ثلاثة" کے موجب پر عمل نہيں ہوتا بلکہ اس کے موجب سے کمی لازم آئے گی کيونکہ طلاق کے وقت طہر کا کچھ نہ کچھ حصہ گزر چکا ہوگا اور بعض طہر باقی ہو گا، تو جب اس کو عدت میں شمار کريں گے تو اس کے بعد والے دو طہر تو مکمل ہوں گے لیکن يہ طہر مکمل نہيں ہوگا، تو عدت دو طہر اور تيسرے طہر کا کچھ حصہ ہوئی، تو "ثلاثة" کے موجب پر عمل نہ ہوا۔ اور اگر طلاق والے طہر کو عدت میں شمار نہ کيا جائے بلکہ اس کے بعد تين طہر عدت میں شمار کيے جائيں، تو بھی "ثلاثة" کے موجب پر عمل نہيں ہو گا بلکہ عدت اس سے زيادہ لازم آئے گی کيونکہ تين طہر بعد والے ہو جائيں گے اور کچھ طہر يہ بھی ہو جائے گا جس میں طلاق ہوئی ہے، تو يہ تين سے بڑھ گئے، لہذا "ثلاثة" کے موجب پر عمل نہ ہوا۔ اس لیے "قروء" سے طہر مراد لينا درست نہيں، بلکہ "قروء" سے مراد حيض ہے، اس صورت میں "ثلاثة" کے موجب پر عمل ہو جائے گا کيونکہ جس طہر میں طلاق ہوگی اس کے بعد والے متصل تين حيض عدت شمار کر ليے جائيں گے۔ اس صورت میں "ثلاثة" سے نہ کمی ہوگی نہ زيادتی۔
دليل امام شافعی: امام شافعی کی دليل ديتے ہيں کہ يہ ضابطہ ہے کہ اگر عدد مذکر ہو تو تميز مؤنث آتی ہے، اور اگر عدد مؤنث ہو تو تميز مذکر آتی ہے۔ اور يہاں عدد "ثلاثة" ہے جو مؤنث ہے، اس لیے تميز یعنی "قروء" سے وہی معنی مراد ہوگا جو مذکر ہے، اور وہ طہر ہے نہ کہ حيض، کيونکہ حيض مؤنث معنوی ہے۔ اس لیے يہاں طہر مراد ہے۔
جواب: حيض اگر چہ مؤنث معنوی ہے، لیکن لفظوں کے اعتبار سے يہ مذکر ہے، اس لیے اس کو مراد لينے میں کوئی حرج نہيں ہے۔ اس سے ضابطہ کی خلاف ورزی لازم نہيں آتی۔
اعتراض: طہر مراد لينے کی صورت میں آپ نے جو خرابی بيان کی ہے وہ حيض مراد لينے کی صورت میں بھی پائی جا سکتی ہے۔ وہ اس طرح کہ اگر کوئی آدمی اپنی بيوی کو حيض میں طلاق ديتا ہے تو يہ حيض عدت میں شمار کيا جائے گا يا نہيں؟ اگر شمار کيا جائے تو پھر عدت تين حيض سے کم ہوگی، اور اگر شمار نہ کيا جائے بلکہ بعد والے تين حيض شمار کيے جائيں تو تين حيض سے زيادہ عدت ہو جائے گی، لہذا خرابی تو اس صورت میں بھی لازم آتی ہے۔
جواب (1): اصل میں مسنون طلاق وہ ہے جو طہر میں دی جائے، اور ہم اسی کی بات کر رہے ہيں، اور مسنون طلاق میں عدت تين حيض ہی بنتی ہے کم يا زيادہ نہيں بنتی کيونکہ جس طہر میں طلاق ہوگی اس کے متصل بعد والے تين حيض عدت شمار کر ليے جائيں گے۔
جواب (2): اگر حيض میں طلاق دی جائے تو بھی خاص (ثلاثة) کے موجب میں کمی زيادتی لازم نہيں آتی، کيونکہ ہم کہتے ہيں کہ جس حيض میں طلاق ہوتی ہے اس حيض کو عدت میں شمار کيا جائے گا لیکن مکمل نہيں بلکہ جتنا حيض باقی ہے اتنا ہی شمار کيا جائے گا، اور جتنے ايام گزر چکے ہيں ان کو بعد میں پورا کيا جائے گا، جس کی صورت يہ ہے کہ ان بقيہ ايام کے بعد عورت دو حيض مکمل گزارے گی، اور اس کے بعد جو حيض آئے گا اس میں سے اتنے دن شمار کيے جائيں گے جتنے پہلے حيض (طلاق والے حيض) میں سے رہ گئے تھے، تو اس طرح تين حيض مکمل ہو جائيں گے، البتہ اس عورت پر عدت گزارنے کا حکم اس وقت لگايا جائے گا جب يہ آخری حيض گزر جائے گا، کيونکہ حيض تجزی کو قبول نہيں کرتا جس طرح باندی کی عدت ويسے تو ڈیڑھ حيض ہونی چاہیے لیکن حيض کی تجزی قبول نہ کرنے کی وجہ سے اس کی عدت دو حيض مکمل رکھی گئی ہے۔ تو اسی طرح يہاں بھی ہے، عدت گزرنے کا حکم اس وقت ہوگا جب تيسرا حيض ختم ہوگا۔
بعض طہر مکمل طہر نہيں ہو سکتا
على ان بعض الظهر ليس بظهر: يہاں سے ایک اعتراض کا جواب ہے۔
And the intended meaning for us is the general meaning: From here it is said that the negation of probability in the specific (khass) is the same as the negation of probability in the general (amm); that is, the negation of probability arising from a proof (dalil) is intended, not the negation of absolute probability. So the meaning is: in the specific, there is no such probability that arises from a proof, but absolute probability can exist, i.e., probability without proof can be found.
First Branch on the Ruling of Khass
In His statement, the Most High: {And divorced women shall wait for themselves for three quru'} – it does not carry quru' as tuhr: From here the first branch on the ruling of khass is explained, so before that understand that the word 'qur'' is homonymous; it has two meanings: hayd (menstruation) and tuhr (purity). Now Allah's blessed saying: {And divorced women shall wait for themselves for three quru'}. In this, Imam Abu Hanifa takes 'quru'' to mean hayd; he says that the 'iddah of a divorced woman is counted by menstruations. And Imam al-Shafi'i takes 'quru'' to mean tuhr; he says that the woman's 'iddah is counted by pure periods.
Branch: Imam [Abu Hanifa] says that the word 'thalathah' (three) in 'thalathatu quru'' is specific (khass). It indicates a specific number which is above two and below four. And because the specific makes the ruling obligatory definitively, therefore here the meaning of 'quru'' must be taken such that the requirement of the word 'thalathah' is fulfilled. And that is only possible when 'quru'' is taken to mean hayd. If tuhr is intended, then the requirement of 'thalathah' cannot be fulfilled, and that is because the recommended divorce (talaq masnun) occurs during tuhr. Now if a man divorces his wife during tuhr, is this tuhr in which the divorce occurred counted among the 'iddah's tuhr periods? If it is counted, then the requirement of 'thalathah' is not fulfilled; rather, a deficiency from its requirement would occur because at the time of divorce, some part of the tuhr has passed and some part remains. So when it is counted in the 'iddah, the two subsequent tuhr periods would be complete, but this tuhr would not be complete; so the 'iddah would be two tuhr periods plus a part of the third tuhr, so the requirement of 'thalathah' would not be fulfilled. And if the tuhr of divorce is not counted in the 'iddah, but rather three subsequent tuhr periods are counted, then also the requirement of 'thalathah' would not be fulfilled; rather, the 'iddah would be more than that, because three later tuhr periods would be added and there would also be the tuhr in which the divorce occurred, so it would exceed three. Hence, taking 'quru'' to mean tuhr is not correct. Rather, 'quru'' must mean hayd; in that case the requirement of 'thalathah' would be fulfilled, because after the tuhr in which divorce occurs, the immediately following three menstruations would be counted as 'iddah. In this case, there would be neither deficiency nor excess from 'thalathah'.
Proof of Imam al-Shafi'i: They give Imam al-Shafi'i's proof that it is a rule: if the number is masculine, the distinguishing word (tamyiz) is feminine; and if the number is feminine, the tamyiz is masculine. And here the number 'thalathah' is feminine, so the tamyiz, i.e., 'quru'', must have a meaning that is masculine, and that is tuhr, not hayd, because hayd is feminine in meaning. Therefore, tuhr is intended here.
Response: Hayd, although feminine in meaning, is masculine in terms of its word-form; therefore there is no harm in taking it as intended. This does not necessitate a violation of the rule.
Objection: The defect you mentioned when taking tuhr as intended can also be found when taking hayd as intended. In this way: if a man divorces his wife during hayd, is this hayd counted in the 'iddah or not? If it is counted, then the 'iddah would be less than three menstruations; and if it is not counted but rather the subsequent three menstruations are counted, then the 'iddah would be more than three menstruations. Therefore, the defect also occurs in this case.
Answer (1): Basically, the recommended divorce (talaq masnun) is that which is given during tuhr, and we are speaking only about that. In the recommended divorce, the 'iddah is exactly three menstruations, no less and no more, because after the tuhr in which divorce occurs, the immediately following three menstruations are counted as 'iddah.
Answer (2): If divorce is given during hayd, then too there is no deficiency or excess in the requirement of the specific (thalathah), because we say: the hayd in which divorce occurs is counted in the 'iddah, but not completely; rather, as much of the hayd as remains is counted, and the days that have passed will be made up later. The method is: after those remaining days, the woman will complete two full menstruations, and then in the next menstruation, as many days will be counted as were left from the first hayd (the hayd of divorce). Thus, three menstruations will be completed. However, the ruling that the woman has completed her 'iddah will be given when this last menstruation ends, because hayd does not admit of fractionation (tajazzi), just as the 'iddah of a slave-girl should be one and a half menstruations, but because hayd does not accept fractionation, her 'iddah is kept as two full menstruations. So similarly here: the ruling of completion of 'iddah occurs when the third menstruation ends.
Some part of tuhr cannot be a complete tuhr
On the basis that some of the tuhr is not tuhr: From here a response to an objection.
Source pages p. 537

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools over the interpretation of the word 'qur'' in the Quranic verse regarding the 'iddah of divorced women. The author begins by clarifying a principle about the specific (khass): the negation of probability in the specific is the negation of probability arising from a proof, not absolute probability. Then he presents the first branch (tafri') concerning the ruling of khass. The word 'qur'' is homonymous, meaning both menstruation (hayd) and purity (tuhr). Abu Hanifa holds that 'qur'' means hayd, so the 'iddah is counted by three menstruations. Al-Shafi'i holds it means tuhr, so the 'iddah is counted by three purity intervals. The Hanafi argument focuses on the specific number 'thalathah' (three). Since 'thalathah' is a specific number (khass) that obligates a definite count, the meaning of 'qur'' must be chosen such that the count of exactly three is possible. If 'qur'' means tuhr, then the 'iddah cannot be exactly three complete tuhr periods because the divorce occurs during a tuhr, making that tuhr incomplete. Whether you count that tuhr or not, the count becomes either less than three or more than three, violating the requirement of 'thalathah'. If 'qur'' means hayd, then the 'iddah is exactly three complete menstruations after the tuhr of divorce, fulfilling the number exactly. Next, the author presents the Shafi'i proof based on the grammatical rule of number-gender agreement: the number 'thalathah' is feminine, so its tamyiz should be masculine, and 'qur'' in the masculine sense means tuhr (hayd is feminine in meaning). The Hanafi response is that while hayd is feminine in meaning, its word-form is masculine, so it can be used as tamyiz for a feminine number without violating the rule. Then an objection is raised: the same defect of incomplete count could occur if divorce happens during hayd, if hayd is taken as the meaning. The Hanafi gives two answers: (1) the discussion is about recommended divorce (talaq sunni) which occurs during tuhr, not during hayd; (2) even if divorce occurs during hayd, the Hanafi method of counting hayd with a make-up for the partial hayd ensures exactly three menstruations, because hayd does not admit fractionation — similar to the 'iddah of a slave-girl. Finally, the author adds a brief note: 'some part of tuhr cannot be a complete tuhr', which is an additional response to an objection.

Key Terms
قرء (qur'): A homonymous word meaning both menstruation (hayd) and purity (tuhr); the dispute revolves around which meaning is intended in the Quranic verse.
خاص (khass): A word that indicates a specific defined meaning, with no generalization; here the number 'thalathah' is khass because it indicates exactly three.
ثلاثة (thalathah): The number three; in the verse, it is a specific number (khass) that must be fulfilled exactly.
تمييز (tamyiz): The distinguishing word that follows a number; it must agree in gender with the number according to Arabic grammar.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Abu Hanifa interpret 'qur'' as menstruation (hayd) rather than purity (tuhr) in the verse about 'iddah?
A) Because the word 'qur'' is grammatically masculine.
B) Because the specific number 'thalathah' requires a meaning that yields exactly three complete units, and only hayd does so.
C) Because the Prophet (peace be upon him) said so.
D) Because the majority of scholars interpret it that way.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school interprets 'qur'' as hayd (menstruation) because the specific number 'thalathah' requires an exact count of three complete units, which only hayd can provide.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
8 min
The Linguistic Rule in the Quru' Dispute
Core linguistic dispute between Hanafi and Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Shafi'i linguistic argument based on number-gender agreement and explain the Hanafi response regarding the word 'hayd' being masculine in form.

Core Idea
The gender agreement rule is not absolute: a word's grammatical gender can override its semantic gender.

The dispute over the meaning of 'quru'' in the iddah verse (Quran 2:228) is a classic example of how usul al-fiqh principles, specifically linguistic rules, shape fiqh rulings. The Shafi'i school argues that the number-gender rule forces the meaning to be 'tuhr' (purity period), while the Hanafi school counters that 'hayd' (menstruation) is grammatically masculine and thus satisfies the rule. Understanding this linguistic argument is essential to grasp the root of the difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Shafi'i Rule
Read the Shafi'i argument: if the number is feminine, the specification (tamyiz) must be masculine. Here 'thalathah' is feminine, so 'quru'' must be masculine in meaning. Since 'tuhr' is masculine and 'hayd' is semantically feminine, 'quru'' means tuhr.
This is a well-known qa'idah lughawiyyah.
2
Apply the Rule to Quru'
Note that the number 'thalathah' (three) is grammatically feminine. According to the rule, the tamyiz (specification) must be masculine. The Shafi'i conclude that 'quru'' must refer to something masculine, i.e., tuhr.
Tuhr (purity) is masculine in Arabic.
3
Understand the Hanafi Response
The Hanafi text responds that 'hayd' is grammatically masculine (لفظاً مذكر), even if its meaning is feminine. Therefore, it can be the intended tamyiz without violating the rule.
Key: grammatical gender versus semantic gender.
4
Note the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafi iddah ends after three menstruations; Shafi'i iddah ends after three purity periods, which can be shorter in duration.
This difference matters in real-life legal rulings.

دلیل امام شافعی:۔ امام شافعی کی دلیل دیتے ہیں کہ یہ ضابطہ ہے کہ اگر عدد ذکر ہو تو تمیز مونٹ آتی ہے اور اگر عد د مونث ہو تو تمیز یذکر آتی ہے۔ اور یہاں عدد ثلثہ ہے جو مونث ہے اس لیے تمیز یعنی قروء سے وہی معنی مراد ہوگا۔ جو مذکر ہے۔ اور وہ طہر ہے نہ کہ حیض کیونکہ حیض مونث معنوی ہے اس لیے یہاں طہر مراد ہے۔ جواب:۔ حیض اگر چه مونث معنوی ہے لیکن لفظوں کے اعتبار سے یہ مذکر ہے اس لیے اس کو مراد لینے میں کوئی حرج نہیں ہے۔ اس سے ضابطہ کی خلاف ورزی لازم نہیں آتی۔
اعتراض: طہر مراد لینے کی صورت میں آپ نے جو خرابی بیان کہ ہے وہ حیض مراد لینے کی صورت میں بھی پائی جاسکتی ہے۔ وہ اس طرح کہ اگر کوئی آدمی اپنی بیوی کو حیض میں طلاق دیتا ہے تو یہ حیض عدت میں شمار کیا جائے گا یا نہیں اگر شمار کیا جائے تو پھر عدت تین حیض سے کم ہوگی اور اگر شمار نہ کیا جائے بلکہ بعد والے تین حیض شمار کئے جائیں تو تین حیض سے زیادہ عدت ہو جائے گی لہذ اخرابی تو اس صورت میں بھی لازم آتی ہے۔
جواب (۱):۔ اصل میں مسنون طلاق وہ ہے جو طہر میں دی جائے اور ہم اسی کی بات کر رہے ہیں اور مسنون طلاق میں عدت تین حیض ہی بنتی ہے کم یا زیادہ نہیں بنتی کیونکہ جس طہر میں طلاق ہوگی اس کے متصل بعد والے تین حیض عدت شمار کر لیے جائیں گے۔
جواب (۲):- اگر حیض میں طلاق دی جائے تو بھی خاص (ثلثہ ) کے موجب میں کمی زیادتی لازم نہیں آتی کیونکہ ہم کہتے ہیں کہ جس حیض میں طلاق ہوتی ہے اس حیض کو عدت میں شمار کیا جائے گا لیکن مکمل نہیں بلکہ جتنا حیض باقی ہے اتنا ہی شمار کیا جائے گا اور جتنے ایام گزر چکے ہیں ان کو بعد میں پورا کیا جائے گا جس کی صورت یہ ہے کہ ان بقیہ ایام کے بعد عورت دو حیض مکمل گزارے گی اور اس کے بعد جو حیض آئے گا اس میں سے اتنے دن شمار کئے جائیں گے جتنے پہلے حیض (طلاق والے حیض) میں سے رہ گئے تھے تو اس طرح تین حیض مکمل ہو جائیں گے البتہ اس عورت پر عدت گزار نے کا حکم اس وقت لگایا جائے گا جب یہ آخری حیض گزر جائے گا کیونکہ حیض تجزی کو قبول نہیں کرتا جس طرح باندی کی عدت ویسے تو ڈیڑھ حیض ہونی چاہیے لیکن حیض کی تجزی قبول نہ کرنے کی وجہ سے اس کی عدت دو حیض مکمل رکھی گئی ہے۔ تو اسی طرح یہاں بھی ہے۔ عدت گزرنے کا حکم اس وقت ہوگا جب تیسرا حیض ختم ہوگا۔
بعض طہر مکمل طہر نہیں ہو سکتا
على ان بعض الظهر ليس بطبر :۔ یہاں سے ایک اعتراض کا جواب ہے۔

اعتراض: طہر مراد لینے کی صورت میں آپ نے جو خرابی بیان کی ہے ہم کہتے ہیں کہ وہ خرابی لازم نہیں آتی کیونکہ طہر اس گھڑی کا نام ہے جس میں عورت حیض سے پاک ہو یعنی کم از کم وہ وقت جس پر طہر کا اطلاق ہو سکے وہ طہر ہے اور وہ ایک گھڑی ہے یہ ضروری نہیں کہ مکمل ایام جو دو حیفوں کے درمیان ہوں وہ ایک مکمل طہر ہے اور اگر کچھ ایام گزرجائیں تو بقیہ ایام بعض طہر ہوں گے تو اب ہم کہتے ہیں کہ جس طہر میں طلاق دی جائے گی اس کو عدت میں شمار کیا جائے گا چونکہ بعض طہر کو بھی طہر ہی کہا جاتا ہے اس لیے یہاں یہ نہیں کہا جائے گا کہ یہ بعض طہر ہے بلکہ یہ مکمل طہر ہے اور مکمل طہر ہی عدت شمار ہوگا جب مکمل طہر عدت شمار ہورہا ہے تو عدت مکمل تین طہر ہوئی کم نہ ہوئی تو ثلثہ کے موجب پر عمل ہو گیا۔
جواب (۱):۔ اس عتراض کے دو جواب دیئے ہیں۔ ایک جمہور نے دیا ہے اور ایک مائن نے جمہور نے یہ جواب دیا ہے کہ آپ کے اعتراض سے معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ ہر وہ گھڑی جس میں عورت حیض سے پاک رہے گی وہ مکمل طہر شمار ہو گی تو مطلب یہ ہو گا کہ دو حیضوں کے درمیان وقفہ آپ کے نزدیک ایک طہر نہیں ہے بلکہ کئی طہر ہیں یعنی جتنی گھڑیاں ہیں اتنے طہر ہیں اور اس سے یہ لازم آئے گا کہ خاوند جب بیوی کو طہر میں طلاق دے گا اس کے بعد متصل ہر گھڑی ایک طہر شمار ہوگا تو جتنی گھڑیاں متحقق ہونگی اتنے طہر متحقق ہونگے تو اس سے یہ لازم آئے گا کہ خاوند جب بیوی کو طہر میں طلاق دے گا اس کے بعد متصل ہر گھڑی ایک طہر شمار ہوگا تو تین گھڑیاں گزرنے سے تین طہر گزر جائیں گے تو گویا تین گھڑیاں گزرنے سے آپ کے نزدیک عورت کی عدت گزر جائے گی پھر تو یہ کہنا چاہیے کہ عورت کی عدت طلاق کے بعد تین گھڑیاں ہیں نہ یہ کہ اس مکمل طہر کے بعد جب حیض آئے گا اور اس کے بعد دو ہر اور گزریں گے تو پھر عدت مکمل ہو گی لیکن آپ اس بات کے قائل نہیں ہیں لہذا آپ کا یہ کہنا کہ بعض طہر بھی مکمل طہر ہی ہوتا ہے درست نہیں ہے۔
جواب (۲):۔ مائن نے جواب دیا کہ بعض طہر مکمل طہر نہیں ہوتا کیونکہ اگر ایسا ہو تو چونکہ پہلے اور تیسرے طہر میں کوئی فرق نہیں ہے اس لیے اگر پہلے طہر کا بعض حصہ مکمل طہر شمار ہو سکتا ہے تو تیسرے طہر کا بعض حصہ بھی مکمل طہر شمار ہوسکتا ہے تو اب ہم یہ کہیں گے کہ اس پہلے طہر (طلاق والے ظہر اور اس کے بعد دوسرے طہر کو گزارنے کے بعد جب تیسر اطہر آئے گا تو اس تیسرے طہر کا کچھ حصہ گزرنے کے بعد عورت کی عدت پوری ہو جانی چاہیے حالانکہ یہ اجتماع کے خلاف ہے لہذا یہ کہنا کہ بعض ہر مکمل طہر ہی ہوتا ہے درست نہ ہوا۔
جمہور و مائن کے جوابات میں فرق
فرق بین الجوابین:۔ جمہور اور مائن کے جواب میں فرق یہ ہے کہ جمہور کو یہ جواب دیا جاسکتا ہے کہ جناب ہمارا مطلب یہ
Al-Tarwih li-Halli al-Tawdih wa al-Talwih. Proof of Imam Shafi'i: Imam Shafi'i argues that there is a rule: if the number is masculine, the specification (tamyiz) is feminine; and if the number is feminine, the specification is masculine. Here the number 'thalathah' (three) is feminine, so the specification, i.e., 'quru'', will mean what is masculine. That is tuhr (purity), not hayd (menstruation) because hayd is semantically feminine. So here tuhr is intended. Response: Hayd although semantically feminine, is masculine in terms of wording, so there is no objection to taking it as intended. This does not violate the rule. Objection: The defect you mentioned when taking tuhr as meaning can also be found when taking hayd as meaning. That is, if a man divorces his wife during hayd, is that hayd counted in the iddah or not? If counted, then the iddah would be less than three hayds; if not counted, and instead the three subsequent hayds are counted, then the iddah would exceed three hayds. So the defect arises in that case too. Answer (1): Essentially, the prescribed (masnun) divorce is that given during tuhr, and we are discussing that. In masnun divorce, the iddah is exactly three hayds, no less or more, because the three hayds immediately after that tuhr are counted. Answer (2): If divorce is given during hayd, deficiency or excess does not necessarily follow from the requirement of 'thalathah', because we say that the hayd in which the divorce occurred is counted in the iddah, but not fully; only the remaining part of that hayd is counted, and the days that have passed are made up later. The method is: after those remaining days, the woman will complete two full hayds, and then when the next hayd comes, from it as many days will be counted as were left from the first hayd (the one of divorce). Thus three hayds will be completed. However, the ruling of completing the iddah is given when this last hayd ends, because hayd does not tolerate fragmentation (tajazzi). Just as for the iddah of a slave girl, it should ideally be one and a half hayds, but due to hayd's non-fragmentation, her iddah is set at two full hayds. Similarly here, the ruling of iddah completion is when the third hayd ends. Some tuhr cannot be a complete tuhr. 'Ala anna ba'd al-tuhr laysa bi-tuhr: This is a response to an objection. Objection: The defect you mentioned when taking tuhr as meaning – we say that defect does not necessarily arise, because tuhr is the name of the moment when a woman is pure from hayd, i.e., the minimum time on which the term tuhr can be applied is a moment. It is not necessary that the complete days between two hayds are one complete tuhr; if some days pass, the remaining days are part of tuhr (ba'd al-tuhr). Now we say that the tuhr in which divorce is given will be counted in the iddah. Since some tuhr is also called tuhr, it will not be said that this is some tuhr; rather it is a complete tuhr, and a complete tuhr is counted as iddah. When a complete tuhr is counted as iddah, the iddah becomes three complete tuhrs, no decrease, so the requirement of 'thalathah' is fulfilled. Answer (1): To this objection, two answers are given. One by the Jamhur (majority) and one by Main. The Jamhur answered: Your objection indicates that every moment a woman is free from hayd is considered a complete tuhr. Then it would mean that the interval between two hayds is not one tuhr but many tuhrs – as many moments, so many tuhrs. From this it would follow that when a husband divorces his wife during tuhr, immediately after that every moment would count as a tuhr. So as many moments occur, so many tuhrs occur. Then three moments passing would mean three tuhrs have passed. So in your view, the woman's iddah would pass with three moments. Then it should be said that the woman's iddah after divorce is three moments, not that after the complete tuhr, when hayd comes, and then two more tuhrs pass, then the iddah is complete. But you do not believe that. Therefore, your saying that some tuhr is also a complete tuhr is incorrect. Answer (2): Main answered that some tuhr is not a complete tuhr because if it were, then since there is no difference between the first and third tuhr, if part of the first tuhr can be counted as a complete tuhr, then part of the third tuhr could also be counted as a complete tuhr. So we would say: after the first tuhr (the one of divorce) and after the second tuhr passes, when the third tuhr comes, after part of that third tuhr passes the woman's iddah should be complete. But this is against consensus. Therefore, the claim that some tuhr is always a complete tuhr is incorrect. Difference between the answers of Jamhur and Main: The difference between the two answers is that the Jamhur can be given this reply: Our intention is...

Commentary
This passage presents a classic usul al-fiqh dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the interpretation of the word 'quru'' (plural of qur') in the verse on iddah (Quran 2:228). The Shafi'i position, as presented first, relies on a linguistic rule (qā‘idah) about number-gender agreement between a number and its specification (tamyīz). Since the number 'thalāthah' is feminine, the specification should be masculine; thus 'quru'' must refer to something masculine. The Shafi'is argue that hayd (menstruation) is semantically feminine, so the masculine meaning is tuhr (purity period). The Hanafi response counters that although hayd is semantically feminine, the word itself is masculine in form, so it can serve as the specification without violating the rule. The text then presents an objection from the opponent: if tuhr is taken as meaning, a similar defect (deficiency or excess in the iddah count) arises when divorce occurs during tuhr. The Hanafi defense is two-fold: first, the discussion is about masnūn (sunna-compliant) divorce given during tuhr, where the iddah of three hayds is preserved. Second, for divorce during hayd, the Hanafis offer a detailed fragmentation (tajazzī) solution: the remaining part of the current hayd is counted, then two full hayds, and then the shortfall from the first hayd is made up in the third hayd. This preserves the total of three hayds without exceeding it, using the principle that hayd does not tolerate fragmentation (so the iddah is only complete when the third hayd ends). The passage then moves to a second objection: if some tuhr (ba‘ḍ al-ṭuhr) is considered a complete tuhr, absurd consequences follow. Two answers are given: the Jamhur's answer shows that this leads to the conclusion that three moments would complete the iddah, which is not the Shafi'i position. Main's answer shows that if partial tuhr can be complete, then part of the third tuhr would complete the iddah, contradicting consensus. Thus the Shafi'i objection fails. The difference between the two responses is clarified in terms of their logical implications.

Key Terms
قرء (qur' (pl. quru')): The term in the verse interpreted as either hayd (menstruation) or tuhr (purity period).
تمييز (tamyiz): The specificatory noun that differentiates the meaning after a number.
بعض الطهر (ba'd al-tuhr): A portion of the purity interval that is not considered a complete tuhr.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Shafi'i argument regarding the meaning of quru' in the iddah verse?
A) Quru' means hayd because of qiyas.
B) Quru' means tuhr because of the number-gender rule.
C) Quru' can mean either.
D) Quru' is ambiguous and requires ijma.

Key Takeaway
The dispute over quru' exemplifies how differing usul principles (grammatical vs. semantic gender) lead to divergent fiqh rulings, and that both are valid within their respective frameworks.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
10 min
Fragmentation and the Ba'd al-Tuhr Objection
Defense of Hanafi position through fragmentation and refutation of tuhr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi fragmentation (tajazzi) solution to the objection that the iddah could be less than three hayds, and evaluate the responses to the Shafi'i argument that ba'd al-tuhr can be counted as a complete tuhr.

Core Idea
Fragmentation of hayd is not allowed: the entire hayd must be complete, but partial hayd can be compensated by taking a partial from the third hayd.

The Shafi'i objection that the Hanafi position leads to deficiency or excess in the iddah count is answered through a detailed fragmentation method unique to the Hanafi school. Additionally, the Shafi'i argument that a part of the purity period (ba'd al-tuhr) can count as a full tuhr is refuted by two logical responses from the Hanafi commentators. This lesson explores these sophisticated usul arguments.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Shafi'i Objection
The Shafi'i object that if quru' means hayd, then a divorce during hayd could result in the iddah being either less than three hayds (if that hayd is counted) or more (if not counted).
This is the deficiency/excess objection.
2
Hanafi Answer 1: Masnun Divorce
The first Hanafi answer restricts discussion to masnun divorce given during tuhr, where the iddah is exactly three hayds.
This avoids the issue entirely but does not cover all cases.
3
Hanafi Answer 2: Fragmentation for Divorce During Hayd
For the non-ideal case, the Hanafis propose a fragmentation solution: count the remaining part of the current hayd, then two full hayds, then make up the shortfall from the third hayd. This ensures exactly three hayds are counted.
Key principle: hayd does not tolerate fragmentation (tajazzi), so the iddah is complete only when the third hayd ends.
4
The Ba'd al-Tuhr Objection
The Shafi'i argue that if tuhr is the meaning, then a divorce during a purity period – even a part of it – counts as a full tuhr, thus preserving the count.
This challenges the Hanafi claim of deficiency.
5
Hanafi Responses to the Ba'd al-Tuhr Objection
Two responses: (1) Jamhur: if every moment of purity counts as a tuhr, then three moments would complete the iddah, which is absurd. (2) Main: if partial tuhr can be complete, then part of the third tuhr could complete the iddah, contradicting the requirement of three full tuhrs.
Both responses show that ba'd al-tuhr cannot be a complete tuhr.
